Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUAN AUGUSTIN COSUELO-QUINTERO A.K.A. JUAN AUGUSTINE CONSUELO-QUINTERO A.K.A. JUAN AUGUSTIN CONSUELO-QUINTERO
File: AXXX XX9 866 - San Diego, CA
April 22, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Tina N. Malek, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Scott Simpson
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The Board’s order dated October 8, 2008, remanded this matter to the Immigration Judge for further fact-finding. The Immigration Judge’s August 27, 2009, decision on remand denied the respondent’s application for adjustment of status and ordered him removed to Mexico. The Board entered the final administrative order on November 5, 2010, when we summarily dismissed the respondent’s appeal for failure to file a statement of reasons for the appeal and unexplained failure to file a brief.1 The respondent filed this motion to reopen his removal proceedings on January 15, 2013. The motion is filed nearly 2 years after the filing deadline. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i) (90-day time limit for filing motion to reopen). The Department of Homeland Security has filed a memorandum opposing the motion, which will be denied.
 
In bringing this untimely motion to reopen, the respondent claims ineffective assistance of counsel. The Board held in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), that due process rights may be violated when ineffective assistance of counsel prevents an alien from reasonably presenting his case, and the time for filing a motion to reopen may be tolled in cases of ineffectiveness of counsel. The alien must, however, show that he was prejudiced by counsel’s performance. In Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814, 826 (9th Cir. 2003), the Ninth Circuit held that an alien failed to show prejudice when he could not “show that his admissibility arguments might have been successful on appeal to the BIA.” Since prejudice had not been shown, the court rejected an ineffective assistance of counsel claim in that case and upheld the Board’s summary dismissal of the appeal even though counsel failed to file a brief or statement of reasons for the appeal.2
 
Thus, in Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, supra, and Matter of Lozada, supra, counsels’ failure to file statements of reasons or briefs on appeal, although resulting in summary dismissal of the appeals, did not amount to deprivation of due process and was not prejudicial. In each case, the alien received a full and fair hearing at which the Immigration Judge properly considered and evaluated the alien’s evidence and correctly determined the legal issues. The same reasoning applies in the present matter. This respondent, prior to being admitted as a lawful permanent resident, was convicted under the California Penal Code of assault with a deadly weapon; subsequently, he was convicted of using or being under the influence of a controlled substance. The Immigration Judge properly determined that he never lawfully acquired permanent resident status, and was ineligible for waivers under sections 212(c), 212(h) and 212(i) of the Act. See I.J. at 5-6; see also Tr., Oct. 13, 2005, at 3-5. The respondent’s motion, which does not include a proposed appellate brief or offer the arguments that would be contained in a brief, does not persuade the Board that the Immigration Judge’s decision was incorrect or could have been overturned on appeal if counsel had filed a brief, or that the respondent could establish eligibility for any relief in a reopened hearing.
 
*2 The motion to reopen, therefore, does not establish prejudice from counsel’s performance. The respondent’s untimely motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen and motion for a stay are denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit dismissed in part and denied in part the respondent’s petition for review of the Board’s order. Cosuelo-Quintero v. Holder, 497 Fed.Appx. 756 (9th Cir. 2012).

2

The Rojas-Garcia decision distinguished Dearinger ex rel. Volkova v. Reno, 232 F.3d 1042 (9th Cir. 2000), in which the court recognized an ineffective assistance of counsel claim where the alien’s attorney failed to file a timely notice of appeal, resulting in summary dismissal of the appeal. Dearinger found a rebuttable presumption of prejudice, acknowledging that the alien was required to demonstrate a “plausible ground for relief to justify reopening for ineffective assistance of counsel. Id. at 1046. The alien met that standard.

2013 WL 1933447 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FAWZI SALIM NICOLAS
File: AXXX XX5 832 - Boston, MA
April 15, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has filed a timely motion to reopen his removal proceedings on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
A motion to reopen must establish the existence of new, previously unavailable, material evidence. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). The party requesting reopening bears the burden of establishing that reopening is warranted and that the new evidence would likely affect the outcome of the proceedings. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N 464 (BIA 1992). In the context of a claim of ineffective assistance, the alien must establish that prior counsel’s representation was defective and that the deficiencies affected the outcome of the removal proceedings. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
The respondent’s assertions do not demonstrate that reopening is warranted. The attorney who allegedly committed ineffective assistance of counsel did not represent the respondent during the course of his removal proceedings or during a subsequent appeal to the Board.1 Rather, the respondent alleges the attorney committed ineffective assistance during the course of a filing of an I-130 family-based visa petition, an interview associated with the I-130 petition, and the filing of an application for advance parole. These alleged deficiencies took place in the context of separate proceedings which occurred prior to the initiation of removal proceedings against the respondent and which involve matters over which the Immigration Judge and this Board lack jurisdiction.2 The respondent has not demonstrated that his removal proceedings were affected by ineffective assistance of counsel in any respect. Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

We note that the respondent was represented during the initial removal hearings, and the respondent has not accused the attorney who represented him below of ineffective assistance.

2

As the respondent is an arriving alien, the Immigration Judge would also have lacked jurisdiction over any adjustment of status application, even had the I-130 visa petition been approved. See 8 C.F.R. § 1245.2(a)(1)(ii).

IN RE: JUNIOR DUNKLEY

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on January 4, 2013, when we found that the respondent had waived his right to appeal through counsel. The respondent had not established that his waiver was not knowing and intelligent or that he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel, and we declined to accept the appeal by certification. The respondent has filed a timely motion to reconsider, and now asserts that his waiver was not knowing and intelligent because his counsel had not adequately advised him of his rights, including his right to appeal. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent raises an ineffective assistance of counsel argument, but has not shown compliance with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), and this is not a case was inadequate representation is apparent from the record. See also Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1274 (11th Cir. 2005). The Immigration Judge specifically confirmed with the respondent at the outset of the final hearing that the “gentleman seated next to [him]” was his attorney and authorized to speak on his behalf. The respondent’s best language was English. Thereafter, in the respondent’s presence, his counsel indicated that it had been determined that the respondent could not proceed with an application for adjustment of status and that “we will agree to removal in this case.”1 Respondent’s counsel stated that he did not think there were any “available applications for relief” and agreed that the detained respondent wanted to take an order of removal. The Immigration Judge asked whether “this is a final order” and respondent’s counsel answered “yes.” See 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a) (regarding action by an attorney of record).
 
While the respondent now states that his attorney did not explain the outcome of the Immigration Judge’s decision to him and that it came as a “surprise” that his appeal rights had been waived, his present claims in this regard are not supported by affidavit or otherwise. And, of equal significance, the respondent has not provided his prior counsel with any notice of these claims and an opportunity to respond. See id. Further, there is nothing presented with this motion that would cause us to reconsider the prior conclusion that further review of the Immigration Judge’s decision by certification has not been shown to be warranted.2 Under these circumstances, we are not persuaded that reconsideration of our January 14, 2013, decision is warranted. Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The request for a stay is denied as moot.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

The record includes a conviction record for the respondent that would preclude his eligibility for adjustment of status.

2

There is no issue raised regarding the respondent’s removability and the record does not reflect that he is prima facie eligible for relief from removal.

2013 WL 416243 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HILARIO TLASECA SILVA
File: AXXX XX6 275 - Los Angeles, CA
January 22, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Marcelo Gondim, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on February 27, 2009, when the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s March 11, 2008, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen and rescind the January 28, 2008, removal order entered in absentia. On October 9, 2012, the respondent filed the pending motion to reopen. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent does not dispute the fact that his motion is untimely. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). Rather, alleging ineffective assistance of former counsel(s), he requests that the applicable time limits be equitably tolled. However, the respondent has not sufficiently shown patent error nor substantially complied with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, including the requirement that he show prejudice as a result of the alleged ineffective assistance. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, supra; Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003). See also Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (directing Board to apply Lozada standards to ineffective assistance of counsel claims pending the outcome of a rulemaking process). The respondent has submitted no evidence beyond what was previously before both the Immigration Judge and the Board to support his claim that exceptional circumstances, i.e., injuries from a traffic accident, prevented him from attending his scheduled hearing. The submitted insurance report does not reflect the respondent’s alleged injuries. Likewise, he has submitted no evidence to support his alleged claim that he ultimately did appear as scheduled, albeit late.
 
Moreover, on this record, we do not find that the respondent has sufficiently demonstrated due diligence in pursuing the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See Iturribarria v. INS, supra; see, e.g., Singh v. Gonzales, 491 F.3d 1090 (9th Cir. 2007) (explaining that to warrant equitable tolling of a filing deadline, the petitioner must show that he has acted with due diligence). The doctrine of equitable tolling applies when “despite all due diligence, [the party invoking equitable tolling] is unable to obtain vital information bearing on the existence of the claim.” Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176, 1193 (9th Cir. 2001) (internal quotations omitted). Despite his asserted inability to read and speak English, the respondent does not dispute that he was, at the very least, aware of both the Immigration Judge’s and the Board’s decisions in this case. Other than contacting current counsel, for which the respondent provides no dates or details, the respondent has offered no reasonable explanation for waiting more than 3 years from the Board’s final decision in this case to file the present motion.
 
*2 On this record, the respondent has not provided an adequate basis to excuse the untimeliness of his motion. Nor has he demonstrated an exceptional situation that would warrant reopening of these proceedings in the exercise of discretion under the Board’s sua sponte authority. Accordingly, we will enter the following order.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
2013 WL 416243 (BIA)
End of Document

IN RE: SIGIFREDO BUENO-GARCIA

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jonathan Love
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated October 13, 2010, denying his request for a continuance of proceedings pending action on a collateral attack to his criminal conviction. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Immigration Judge sustained the charge of removability based on the respondent’s February 24, 2005, aggravated felony conviction for conspiracy to deliver a controlled substance in violation of Revised Code of Washington §§ 69.50.401(a)(1)(ii) and 69.50.407 (I.J. at 2; Exh. 2). The Immigration further determined that there was not good cause to continue the case pending action in state court on the respondent’s appeal from the denial of his motion to withdraw his guilty plea and vacate his criminal conviction (I.J. at 3-4). The respondent asserts on appeal that the Immigration Judge erred in denying him a continuance of proceedings, argues that his immigration and criminal defense attorneys rendered ineffective assistance, and states that he was not guilty of the drug charge.
 
An Immigration Judge may grant a motion for continuance “for good cause shown.” See 8 C.F.R. §§ 3.29, 240.6; Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354 (BIA 1983). In determining whether an alien has demonstrated “good cause,” an Immigration Judge has broad discretionary authority. See Matter of Sibrun, supra, at 355-56. Indeed, we have held that “an Immigration Judge’s decision denying [a] motion for continuance will not be reversed unless the alien establishes that [the] denial caused him actual prejudice and harm and materially affected the outcome of his case.” Id. at 356-57. A conviction is final for immigration purposes despite the possibility of discretionary review or collateral attack. See Matter of Polanco, 20 I&N Dec. 894 (BIA 1994).
 
We find no error in the Immigration Judge’s decision denying a continuance of proceedings pending resolution in state court of the respondent’s appeal from the denial of his request to collaterally attack his criminal conviction. On the day of the merits hearing, a motion to withdraw the respondent’s guilty plea had been denied by the state court. We agree that a further continuance pending adjudication of a state court appeal was not warranted.
 
*2 As for the respondent’s claims of ineffective assistance, we find no basis to remand for further proceedings. As an initial matter, we note that the respondent has not complied with the evidentiary requirements for asserting a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against his immigration attorney outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988).1 Moreover, he has not established that he was prejudiced by immigration counsel’s actions. Although more than 2 years have passed since the Immigration Judge’s decision, the respondent has not presented any evidence to establish that his state court appeal was successful and that his criminal conviction has been vacated. See Ortiz v. INS, 179 F.3d 1148, 1153 (9th Cir. 1999) (finding that prejudice must be shown for a due process challenge and that prejudice is found when the performance of counsel was so inadequate that it may have affected outcome); Mohsseni Behbahani v. INS, 796 F.2d 249, 251 (9th Cir. 1986) (finding that to prevail on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim there must be a showing of prejudice by counsel’s performance).
 
As for the respondent’s challenge to the actions of his criminal defense attorney and his claim of innocence to the criminal charge, these matters are within the jurisdiction of the state court, not the Immigration Judge or this Board. See Matter of Onyido, 22 I&N Dec. 552, 558 (BIA 1999) (it is impermissible for Immigration Judges or the Board to go behind a conviction and reassess an alien’s guilt or innocence). Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Sharon Hoffman
FOR THE BOARD

1

Under Matter of Lozada, supra, a motion based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel should be supported by an affidavit, reflect whether a complaint had been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities, and include a response to the allegations by former counsel or report counsel’s failure or refusal to respond. Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639.

IN RE: JURATE VIRGYCIO MILUTYTE

January 9, 2013
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Richard Thompson Herman, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Amy L. Sheurman
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Lithuania, has filed a motion to reopen the proceedings and requesting that we reconsider our previous decision, dated August 23, 2012. In the decision, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from a September 9, 2011, Immigration Judge decision denying the respondent a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(i). The motion will be denied.
 
We review the findings of fact, including determinations of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether or not the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
In her motion, the respondent argues that her previous attorney was ineffective in failing to fully develop the record. She argues he failed to have her clarify problems in her testimony concerning a false claim on a student loan application and to have her husband clearly state that he would not travel to Lithuania, but only while his elderly mother was still alive and needed care in the United States.1 She also argues the attorney erred in failing to submit the student loan documentation. We first observe that the respondent has failed to fully comply with the requirements set out in our decision Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Sako v. Gonzales, 434 F.3d 857 (6th Cir. 2006); Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009). The respondent states that she did not file a complaint against her former attorney as he is a good person. However, this does not excuse compliance with the requirements. Whether or not the previous attorney meant well does not alter the purposes of this requirement, including the deterrence of meritless claims and highlighting the standards expected of attorneys in immigration proceedings. Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639-40.
 
Nonetheless, even had the respondent complied with the filing requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, the motion would be denied as she has failed to demonstrate any prejudice from the actions of the previous attorney. See Sako v. Gonzales, supra. The Immigration Judge and this Board rested our denial of relief, in part, on the respondent’s false statement that her daughter was a lawful permanent resident on an application for college financial aid (BIA at 2; I.J. at 13). The respondent has now submitted a printout from the financial aid office indicating that the daughter’s status was recorded as “eligible - non-citizen” and a response from the office stating there was not enough information to confirm the daughter’s noncitizen status (Resp. Mot., Exh. B). The respondent also argues that, at the time of her testimony, she did not clearly recall what information she had provided on the form.
 
*2 However, the printout submitted fails to support the respondent’s argument, as it does not indicate what status she claimed for her daughter. It has never been argued that she indicated her daughter was a citizen. We continue to accept her testimony at the time of the hearing that she indicated that her daughter was a lawful permanent resident (Tr. at 66 (daughter), 96, 130-35). The failure of the previous attorney to properly submit this document is not material to the respondent’s claim, as it does not contradict the respondent’s testimony. Furthermore, while the respondent now wishes to clarify her and her husband’s testimony or supplement the testimony in various ways, this does not indicate that the previous attorney erred in presenting the case.
 
The respondent also argues that the Board failed to apply the proper legal standard and that we failed to consider all of the evidence of record, in particular the letter submitted by a doctor concerning the husband’s psychological health. We disagree. While the respondent may wish that we gave more weight to the letter and its conclusions, it was considered previously (BIA at 2). The husband will face emotional hardship from the separation and does suffer from depression related to the removal process. Although the letter states that separation would result in “exceptional and extremely unusual hardship” to the husband, we continue to find the hardship he will face does not rise to “extreme hardship” as provided for under immigration law. See Matter of Cervantes, 22 I&N Dec.560 (BIA 1999), aff’d 232 F.3d 684 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
The Board and the Immigration Judge also properly considered the husband’s testimony, at the time of the hearing that the husband would remain in the United States, due to the health of his mother (Tr. at 85-86). It follows that he may choose to travel to Lithuania once he no longer needs to care for her, but this is speculative. In any event, despite his depression and lack of language skills, his relocation to his wife’s country is not a hardship that is out of the ordinary for a couple where one member is facing removal. In closing, we also note the respondent’s arguments concerning family unity and the remand of her daughter’s record for possible relief. Nonetheless, this remand was part of our previous decision and was properly considered at that time. Based on the foregoing, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

However, this provides little support for the ineffective assistance of counsel claim and a review of the record reveals that the attorney provided proper representation.

IN RE: JUAN JOSE CHAVEZ-GUZMAN
File: AXXX XX8 172 - Dallas, TX
December 13, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lorena N. Gutierrez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on July 28, 2011, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal. On July 30, 2012, more than 1 year after the Board’s order, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion is untimely, as it was not filed within 90 days of the Board’s final administrative decision. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). In the motion, the respondent argues that his proceedings should be reopened based on the ineffective assistance of counsel by attorney Keith Willeford, who represented him before the Immigration Judge and the Board (the “former counsel”). The respondent argues that the former counsel provided ineffective assistance of counsel by failing to file before the Immigration Judge a proper application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b)(1), in a timely manner, and by failing to request an extension. The respondent argues that he complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), by submitting an affidavit and notifying the former counsel of his ineffective assistance of counsel claims, as well as filing a complaint with the State Bar of Texas (Motion Exhs. B, C, D). The respondent also submitted an application for cancellation of removal and supporting documents, consisting of his birth certificate and identification card, his son’s birth certificate, his father’s certificate of naturalization, and the approval notice of an I-130 visa petition filed by his father (Motion Exhs. G, K).
 
However, the respondent’s motion is untimely as noted above, and the respondent has presented no arguments, and has submitted no evidence, showing how he acted with due diligence during the 1-year period since the Board dismissed his appeal. The United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit has not addressed in a published decision whether the motion time limitations may be equitably tolled in cases claiming ineffective assistance of counsel. See Ramos-Bonilla v. Mukasey, 543 F.3d 216, 219-220 (5th Cir. 2008) (holding that a request for equitable tolling of a time or number-barred motion to reopen on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel is “in essence an argument that the BIA should have sua sponte reopened the proceeding based upon the doctrine of equitable tolling”). An alien seeking equitable tolling of a motion to reopen period based on ineffective assistance of counsel must affirmatively demonstrate how he acted with due diligence during the entire period he seeks to toll, and the respondent has not done so. See, e.g., Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127 (2d Cir. 2008); Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167 (2d Cir. 2006).
 
*2 The respondent urges us to sua sponte reopen his proceedings under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Based on the circumstances presented in this case, we do not find that the respondent’s case presents “exceptional circumstances” that would warrant the Board’s exercise of its discretion to reopen sua sponte. Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, the respondent’s motion will be denied.1
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller

See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1) (providing that a motion to reopen proceedings for the purpose of submitting an application for relief must be accompanied by the appropriate application for relief and “all supporting documentation”).

IN RE: SAMUEL ALAS CARTAGENA
File: AXXX XX5 229 - Chicago, IL
December 13, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Aarti R. Sampat, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 On September 27, 2012, this Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal. The record showed that the respondent, through counsel, waived appeal. Our decision observed that the respondent did not, in filing his appeal, argue that the waiver was not a knowing and intelligent one. Thus, the Immigration Judge’s decision became administratively final upon the respondent’s waiver of the right to appeal. The respondent has filed a timely motion to reconsider, which will be denied.
 
In his motion, the respondent observes that the Immigration Judge’s order did not reflect that the appeal was waived in his case (Mot. at 1-2). The respondent also states that he did not recall waiving appeal in the immigration court and he asserts that the immigration court’s information system confirmed that the appeal in the case was due by July 11, 2012 (Mot. at 1-2). The respondent argues that appeal was not waived in his case (Mot. at 3-4). In the alternative, he argues that any waiver was not knowingly or intelligently made since his attorney did not explain his appeal rights to him (Mot. at 3-4).
 
The transcript in this matter establishes that the respondent was granted voluntary departure, the only form of relief for which he applied (Tr. at 45-49).1 The respondent does not currently dispute that the attorney who represented him at his hearing stated on the record that appeal would be waived. (Tr. at 49). Although the respondent now asserts that his attorney did not explain his appeal rights to him, the respondent has not filed a motion alleging ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Accordingly, he has not established a basis for reconsideration or reopening. See Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986) (providing that an alien is generally bound by the conduct of his attorney).
 
We separately note that even if the respondent had not waived his right to appeal, an appeal from a decision that granted the only relief that was requested is subject to a summary dismissal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(2)(i)(C).
 
The respondent’s motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
David B. Holmes

IN RE: ELLIOT MUDIAGA ONOBRAKPEYA
File: AXXX XX3 882 - Atlanta, GA
December 10, 2012
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Richard C. Gordon, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The Immigration Judge’s decision dated February 28, 1991, denied the respondent’s application for suspension of deportation and ordered him deported to Nigeria. The respondent, through counsel, filed a timely Notice of Appeal with the Board. Counsel subsequently failed to file a brief or otherwise present any specific factual or legal basis for the appeal, and the Board summarily dismissed the appeal in our decision entered on January 16, 1992. The respondent filed a motion to reopen these proceedings on August 23, 2012. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion, which will be denied.
 
The motion, filed more than 20 years after the Board’s order in the matter, is barred by the applicable time limit. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (motion to reopen deportation proceedings must be filed no later than 90 days after the final deportation order). The motion does not cite any exception to the filing deadline, but claims that the respondent’s former counsel was ineffective in his handling of the appeal decided by the Board in 1992 (incorrectly asserting, however, that counsel did not file a Notice of Appeal). We note that the United States Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit has held that the 90-day time limit for motions to reopen is “mandatory and jurisdictional” and not subject to equitable tolling, even where a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is raised. See Abdi v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 430 F.3d 1148, 1150 (11th Cir. 2005). We further note that the motion does not show that the respondent has complied with the requirements prescribed by the Board in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639-40 (BIA 1988), nor does it show that the respondent has been diligent in pursuing his rights over the time period of more than 20 years since the Board’s decision in his appeal.
 
The motion is not accompanied by affidavits or evidence in any form. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1) (motion to reopen must be supported by affidavits or other material evidence stating the new facts that would be proven at a reopened hearing). Without an evidentiary submission, the motion does not demonstrate either that counsel’s representation was ineffective or that the respondent is eligible for any relief. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 561-62 (BIA 2003) (holding that alien asserting ineffective assistance of counsel must document that he is eligible for the relief sought). Further, while the respondent claims to be eligible for relief, the motion does not include an application for relief as required by 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1).
 
*2 For the above reasons, the respondent’s motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen and motion for a stay are denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion for change of venue is denied as moot.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

2012 WL 6641809 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANTHONY AFUNWA
File: AXXX XX9 725 - Bloomington, MN
December 4, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Herbert A. Igbanugo, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Thomas S. Madison
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 This case was last before us on July 16, 2012, at which time we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny his petition to remove the conditional nature of his residence in the United States under section 216(c)(3)(D) of the Act. The respondent has now filed a timely motion to reopen proceedings on October 15, 2012. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the motion, which will be denied.
 
A motion to reopen shall not be granted unless it appears that the evidence sought to be offered “was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing.” See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). Further, this Board has held that a party who seeks to reopen proceedings to pursue a discretionary grant of relief from removal bears a “heavy burden” of demonstrating that if his motion to reopen were granted, the new evidence presented would likely change the result in the case. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992).
 
With his motion, the respondent has submitted over 30 affidavits from friends and acquaintances as support for his claim that he and his wife entered into a marriage in good faith. We find these statements and affidavits insufficient to bear the respondent’s heavy burden to show that such evidence would likely change the result in his case. Id. The various statements are from witnesses not subject to cross-examination; moreover, as the DHS argues in its opposition, such evidence was previously available during the respondent’s hearing. While the respondent states that his attorney should have told him to obtain such statements, he has not prepared an ineffective assistance of counsel claim in accordance with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), nor has he otherwise demonstrated how his attorney’s advice, or lack thereof, excepts him from this regulatory requirement. See Fongwo v. Gonzales, 430 F.3d 944 (8th Cir. 2005)(attorney’s alleged advice to forego presentation of documents does not mean they were unavailable).1
 
Lastly, we do not find that the respondent’s evidence presents an exceptional circumstance which would warrant reopening in our sua sponte authority. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997)(stating that “[t]he power to reopen on our own motion is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship”). As we noted in our previous decision, even without the respondent’s adverse credibility finding, the DHS proved by a preponderance of the evidence that the respondent did not enter the marriage in good faith (Bd. Dec. at 2; I.J. at 6). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

1

The respondent’s attempt to distinguish himself from Fongwo due to the fact that his affidavits were not yet prepared is an immaterial distinction.

2012 WL 6641784 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OSCAR ARNULFO ALAS-AGUILAR
File: AXXX XX0 139 - Los Angeles, California
November 15, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Reynold E. Finnegan, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a 26-year-old male native and citizen of El Salvador, has appealed from an Immigration Judge’s July 30, 2012, written order which denied his motion to rescind his in absentia removal order. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including the credibility determination, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues involving questions of law, judgement and discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On October 5, 2005, the respondent was placed in removal proceedings with the issuance of a Notice to Appear, which he signed. He failed to appear at a hearing on November 7, 2007, and was ordered removed in absentia. The respondent filed a motion to rescind his in absentia order, claiming that he had not received notice of his hearing, and an Immigration Judge granted the motion on February 16, 2011.
 
On June 8, 2011, the respondent personally appeared, along with prior counsel, and was informed that his next hearing was on March 23, 2012. Following a hearing conducted in absentia on March 23, 2012, when he failed to appear, an Immigration Judge found the respondent subject to removal as charged, and ordered him removed from the United States. See generally section 240 of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a.
 
An order of removal issued after in absentia proceedings may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances, because he did not receive proper notice of the hearing, or because he was in Federal or State custody. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995); Matter of Gonzalez-Lopez, 20 I&N Dec. 644 (BIA 1993). The term “exceptional circumstances” refers to exceptional circumstances beyond the control of the alien, such as serious illness of the alien or death of an immediate relative, but not including less compelling circumstances. Section 240(e)(1) of the Act.
 
On July 17, 2012, the respondent, through present counsel, filed a motion to rescind his March 23, 2012, in absentia order, claiming that he had not received notice of the March 23, 2012, hearing from his prior attorney because he was in a “disagreement” with her and she allegedly did not notify him about the March 23, 2012, hearing.1 He also requested reopening because his prior attorney allegedly committed ineffective assistance of counsel without elaboration. Finally, he claimed that he had not received the Notice to Appear.
 
*2 On July 30, 2012, an Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen because the respondent appeared in person at the June 8, 2011, hearing and received personal and written notification of the March 23, 2012, hearing, as well as the consequences for failure to appear.
 
We will affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent’s prior counsel was properly notified about the March 23, 2012, hearing, which constitutes notice to him. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2); Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (notice to alien’s counsel constitutes notice to alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a). He also personally appeared at the hearing on June 8, 2011, and was notified about the hearing scheduled for March 23, 2012. Contrary to his contentions on appeal, the respondent received personal service of the Notice to Appear, and he was fully informed about the consequences for failing to appear because the Notice to Appear, which he signed on October 5, 2005, indicated that he received oral notice in Spanish about the consequences.
 
Insofar as the respondent alleged in his motion that his prior attorney had committed ineffective assistance of counsel, he failed to specify what conduct constituted the basis of his ineffective assistance claim, he did not comply with the standards to demonstrate ineffective assistance of counsel because he had not filed a formal bar complaint against his prior counsel, and he has not specifically raised ineffective assistance as an issue on appeal.2 Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
Finally, the respondent alleged in a July 16, 2012, affidavit attached to his motion that he “was engaged in a criminal matter at the same time” without elaboration and that he “possibly” may have been in custody on March 23, 2012, but “I am unable to recall the dates of my incarceration.” In addition, the brief submitted on appeal claimed for the first time that the respondent was requesting reopening on the basis of unspecified “exceptional circumstances,” an apparent reference to his possible incarceration on March 23, 2012. However, it is inappropriate to raise exceptional circumstances as a basis for reopening for the first time on appeal because the Immigration Judge has not had an opportunity to address it, and we will not consider it now. Moreover, these allegations are insufficient without more to establish that the respondent was unable to appear because he was in custody on March 23, 2012, and he has not provided any documentation on appeal to establish whether he was incarcerated on March 23, 2012. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
*3 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

On March 1, 2012, the respondent’s prior attorney filed a motion to withdraw, which was denied on March 6, 2012. She indicated that the respondent had failed to respond to both telephone calls and letters, and had failed to arrive for scheduled appointments.

2

His contentions for the first time on appeal about how his prior attorney allegedly did not notify him about the hearing because she mailed him letters in 2012 which he did not receive due to an incorrect zip code are irrelevant because he already had been personally notified on June 8, 2011 about his March 23, 2012, hearing, and he failed to maintain contact with his attorney.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALEMAYEHU MAMO
File: AXXX XX0 452 - Arlington, VA
November 9, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Nash Fayad, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The final administrative order of removal in these proceedings was entered by the Board on November 13, 2008. On May 30, 2012, the respondent, a native and citizen of Ethiopia, filed the pending motion. The respondent does not dispute the fact that his motion is untimely. Rather, he contends that the applicable time limits should be equitably tolled because of the deficient performance of former counsel(s). The respondent asserts, in part, that former counsel(s) never informed him that his appeal had been dismissed. However, the respondent, who does not dispute that he was aware that an appeal was pending before the Board, has not described or identified what efforts he undertook during the period between the time his appeal was filed and his alleged 2009 discovery that it had been dismissed. Likewise, he has not described or identified what efforts he undertook between his alleged 2009 discovery and the filing of the instant motion 3 years later, other than to hire current counsel, for which he also provides no dates or details.
 
Nor has the respondent sufficiently complied with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, including the requirement that he show prejudice as a result of the alleged ineffective assistance. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, supra. See also Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (directing Board to apply Lozada standards to ineffective assistance of counsel claims pending the outcome of a rulemaking process).
 
Under the circumstances, we do not find equitable tolling of the 90-day-deadline warranted. See also Harris v. Hutchinson, 209 F.3d 325, 330 (4th Cir. 2000) (recognizing that equitable tolling may be appropriate where the litigant was either prevented from asserting his or her claim by some kind of wrongful conduct on the part of the defendant, or extraordinary circumstances beyond the litigant’s control made it impossible to comply with the statutory time limit).
 
On this record, the respondent has not provided an adequate basis to excuse the untimeliness of his motion. Nor has he demonstrated an exceptional situation that would warrant reopening in the exercise of discretion under the Board’s sua sponte authority. Accordingly, the respondent’s motion is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
*2 FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 6641696 (BIA)
End of Document

2012 WL 6641705 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIA LORENZO-RAMIREZ
File: AXXX XX0 481 - Newark, NJ
November 6, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Joseph Zurita, Esquire
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, appeals the Immigration Judge’s August 6, 2012, decision denying her motion to reopen. We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment arising in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo, whereas we review findings of fact in such appeals under a “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent’s motion alleged that her prior attorney’s ineffective assistance led to the Immigration Judge’s September 16, 2011, decision ordering the respondent removed in absentia.
 
In Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), we stated that a motion to reopen based upon ineffective assistance of counsel requires (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the respondent setting forth in detail the agreement between the applicant and her legal representative and what actions were to be taken on her behalf, (2) that the counsel whose reputation is being impugned be informed of the allegations against him or her and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) an indication of whether a complaint had been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities, and if not, why not.
 
Under our de novo standard of review, we conclude that the respondent’s motion to reopen did not comply with Matter of Lozada. In fact, the motion conceded that no compliance had yet been attempted. It instead asserted that evidence of compliance would be submitted later. The respondent submitted no further evidence during the 2 week period between the motion’s filing and the issuance of the Immigration Judge’s decision.
 
Nor has the respondent demonstrated compliance on appeal that would warrant a remand. The respondent has submitted a statement describing the events surrounding the issuance of her in absentia order of removal, see Respondent’s Brief at Tab A, but this statement is deficient, for it is signed but unsworn. Unsworn allegations do not constitute evidence. See INS v. Phinpathya, 464 U.S. 183, 188-89 n.6 (1984); Matter of S-M-, 22 I&N Dec. 49, 51 (BIA 1998). Even if this statement had been sworn, the respondent would not have demonstrated compliance with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, for she has not submitted evidence as to whether prior counsel has been informed of the allegations and given an opportunity to respond and evidence whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities.
 
Although the respondent’s motion did not meet the standards for reopening under Matter of Lozada, supra, a motion can nonetheless be a basis for reopening to apply for a form of relief, such as asylum, if the motion contains evidence of circumstances that have arisen subsequent to the final hearing. See 8 C.F.R § 1003.2(c)(1). The respondent’s motion, however, did not present evidence of changed circumstances or country conditions arising after the respondent’s final hearing.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 6641705 (BIA)
End of Document

APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
*1 A Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) must be filed within 30 calendar days of an Immigration Judge’s oral decision or the mailing of a written decision unless the last day falls on a weekend or legal holiday, in which case the appeal must be received no later than the next working day. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.38(b), (c). The Immigration Judge’s written decision was mailed to the respondent’s counsel in September 2007. The respondent did not file her Notice of Appeal with the Board until more than 5 years later on October 1, 2012. Thus, the appeal is untimely. The Immigration Judge’s August 29, 2007, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen accordingly remains final, and the record will be returned to the Immigration Court without further action.1 See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a), 1003.38, 1003.39, 1240.14 and 1240.15.
 
Because we are dismissing the appeal as untimely, either party wishing to file a motion should follow the following guidelines: If you wish to file a motion to reconsider challenging the finding that the appeal was untimely, you must file your motion with the Board. However, if you are challenging any other finding or seek to reopen your case, you must file your motion with the Immigration Court.2 See Matter of Mladineo, 14 I&N Dec. 591 (BIA 1974); Matter of Lopez, 22 I&N Dec. 16 (BIA 1998). You should also keep in mind that there are strict time and number limits on motions to reconsider and motions to reopen. See sections 240(c)(6)(A) and (B) and 240(c)(7)(A) and (C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(6)(A) and (B) and (c)(7)(A) and (C); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(2), 1003.23(b)(1); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
We separately note that the respondent’s allegations that she was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel are not meaningfully raised, where she has not complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), nor shown prejudice.
 
*2 In light of the foregoing, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action.
 
David B. Holmes

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE MANUEL GOMEZ A.K.A. JOSEPH PFEIFER A.K.A. JOSEPH MANUEL PFEIFER-GOMEZ A.K.A. JOSEPH MANUEL PFEIFER
File: AXXX XX3 904 - Eloy, AZ
October 31, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Marina Alexandrovich, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Alec J. Niziolek
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal on December 20, 2011. He submitted a motion to reopen September 14, 2012, also asking for a stay of removal. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the respondent’s motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion is untimely as it was filed more than 90 days after the Board’s decision. Section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). However, the respondent contends that former counsel provided him with ineffective assistance (Motion at 3). Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2000); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). He also asks that the Board sua sponte reopen proceedings (Motion at 9). 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a).
 
The respondent has submitted a statement averring that he filed a state bar complaint against former counsel, Mr. Bracamonte, and mailed a copy of the same to him. However, as he has not submitted a copy of the complaint, it is unclear what errors he alleged Mr. Bracamonte committed. He has not clarified this omission in his motion. In his statement he appears to contend that Mr. Bracamonte erred by admitting the factual allegations, and conceding removability. However, even assuming that was the basis for his bar complaint, he has not identified any error regarding the factual allegations, nor stated a meritorious ground for contesting the charges of removability (Exhs. 1, 1a). Further, as this Board previously noted, the record contains various exhibits supporting the Immigration Judge’s findings regarding both the factual allegations and the removal charges (Exhs. 2, 9, 10, 13). In this regard, we observe that in the instant motion the respondent concedes, rather than contests, his alienage (Declaration). In sum, given this record, Mr. Bracamonte could have reasonably decided to admit the allegations and concede removability. See Knowles v. Mirzayance, 129 S.Ct. 1411, 1422 (2009) ( “Counsel also is not required to have a tactical reason-above and beyond a reasonable appraisal of a claim’s dismal prospects for success-for recommending that a weak claim be dropped altogether.”); Magallanes-Damian v. INS, 783 F.2d 931, 934 (9th Cir. 1986) (attorney’s decision to forego contesting deportability was a tactical decision that did not rise to the level of ineffective assistance).
 
*2 In addition, the respondent has not shown that Mr. Bracamonte’s factual admissions and failure to challenge the grounds of removability “may have affected the outcome of the proceedings.” Iturribarria v. INS, supra at 899-900. Nor has he shown that there were “plausible grounds” for an alternative outcome. Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814, 826 (9th Cir. 2003). As such, he has not shown that he was prejudiced by Mr. Bracamonte’s actions. Furthermore, we do not find that this case presents exceptional circumstances warranting the exercise of our discretionary authority to sua sponte reopen proceedings. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Therefore, his motion is not exempt from the noted time-bar based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Given this record, neither reopening nor a stay of removal is warranted. Accordingly, the following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: BERNARD AKWASI ACHEAMPONG A.K.A. BENARD ACHEAMPONG A.K.A. BERNARD O. ACHEAMPONG
File: AXXX XX1 747 - Baltimore, MD
October 31, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Polina Tomashevsky, Esquire
*1 This case was last before us on May 14, 2009, at which time we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s June 10, 2008, decision finding him removable as an aggravated felon. The respondent has now filed, on June 8, 2012, an untimely motion to reopen proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion, which will be granted pursuant to our sua sponte authority.
 
The procedural history of this case is as follows: The Immigration Judge initially granted the respondent’s motion to terminate proceedings on February 11, 2005, finding that the respondent was not removable under either section 237(a)(1)(C)(I) nor section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Act. Subsequently, in a decision dated September 11, 2006, the Board reversed the Immigration Judge’s decision, and found that the respondent was removable pursuant to section 237(a)(1)(C)(i) of the Act for having violated his nonimmigrant status. We further found that the respondent’s conviction for resisting arrest constituted a crime of violence under section 101(a)(43)(F) of the Act, and therefore created his removability as an aggravated felon under section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Act. As such, we remanded the record for further proceedings regarding the respondent’s requests for relief from removal, or, in the alternative, for an entry of a final order of removal. The respondent appealed this decision to the United States Court of Appeals for the Fourth Circuit,1 but also appeared before the Immigration Court again, and stated on remand, through his attorney, that “we’re reserving all relief. We don’t have any relief at this moment (Tr. at 64). Accordingly, the Immigration Judge issued a summary order of removal on June 10, 2008, indicating that the respondent had not applied for relief. The respondent appealed this decision on September 26, 2008, arguing that the Immigration Judge had erred in finding the respondent removable as an aggravated felon. On May 14, 2009, we found that because the respondent had appealed to the Fourth Circuit rather than filing a timely motion to reconsider our June 10, 2008, decision, we would decline to revisit our previous decision, and we dismissed the respondent’s appeal (2009 Bd. Dec. at 1). The respondent has now filed a motion, on June 8, 2012, arguing that he received ineffective assistance of counsel during his prior proceedings because his prior counsel did not pursue an adjustment of status application in his behalf, even though he had originally requested such relief prior to the Board’s 2005 remand of his proceedings (Tr. at 2-3, 40).
 
*2 In support of these arguments, the respondent has filed evidence in substantial compliance with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The respondent has also filed evidence in support of his claim that he has maintained an approved visa petition through his brother, with a current priority date, since 2003 (Tr. At 3-4), but that at the remanded hearing in 2007, his attorney stated that no relief was available to the respondent (Tr. at 51, 64). The respondent has also submitted an adjustment of status application, as well as various documents in support of his application for relief.
 
We find that a remand is warranted in our sua sponte authority. It does appear that due to the complicated procedural history of the respondent’s case, his opportunity to demonstrate eligibility for adjustment of status was overlooked at his various hearings. Inasmuch as the respondent does not appear prima facie ineligible for such relief, and given the particular circumstances in the respondent’s case, including the DHS’ lack of opposition, we will reopen the respondent’s proceedings under our sua sponte authority and will provide him a further opportunity to seek relief from removal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Upon remand, both parties may further address the respondent’s statutory and discretionary eligibility for adjustment and any commensurate waivers of inadmissibility. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is granted and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing decision.
 
Neil P. Miller

APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On February 29, 2012, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the June 25, 2010, decision in which the Immigration Judge determined that the respondent had abandoned her application for adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a), for failure to file the application in a timely manner. On June 5, 2012, the respondent filed the instant motion to reopen in which she alleges ineffective assistance of counsel on the part of her former attorney. The Department of Homeland Security has not opposed the motion. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(g). The motion will be granted.
 
The respondent has complied with the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim before the Board. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). She has also offered evidence in support of an application for adjustment of status that reveals her prima facie eligibility for adjustment of status as the parent of a United States citizen at the time of the removal hearing. Upon consideration of the evidence before us, it appears that the respondent suffered prejudice as a result of her former attorney’s handling of her case. Id. at 640.
 
Although the respondent’s motion is untimely by 6 days, in light of the particular circumstances present in this case, the Board will exercise its sua sponte authority and grant this unopposed motion to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Accordingly, the proceedings will be reopened and the record remanded to the Immigration Judge for consideration of the respondent’s application for adjustment of status, and for other action as may be deemed appropriate.

IN RE: ADRIAN KRISHNA BADALOO
File: AXXX XX8 408 - Miami, FL
October 23, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kenneth Panzer, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Vera Hochberg
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 The respondent is a native and citizen of Jamaica. An in absentia order of removal was first entered against him on December 8, 2011. On December 21, 2011, the respondent filed a motion seeking to rescind the court’s order. The motion to reopen was denied, and a motion for reconsideration followed on January 3, 2012. On January 4, 2012, the Immigration Judge denied reconsideration. This appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The Immigration Judge considered the respondent’s statement that he wanted his day in court and did not understand the last decision. Given the respondent’s statements, the Immigration Judge denied reconsideration. We agree with the denial. We acknowledge that when the respondent filed his last motion, he submitted a hand-written statement on his own because his attorney was out of the country. Nonetheless, his motion failed to address the issues in the Immigration Judge’s decision with which he took issue. The federal regulations provide that a motion to reconsider must state the reasons for reconsideration, be supported by pertinent case precedent and be predicated on a legal or factual defect in the most recent decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(2). See also Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). As the respondent’s statements did not meet this standard, the respondent did not establish a sufficient basis to justify reconsideration. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006).
 
We acknowledge that on appeal, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge applied the wrong factors in evaluating the merits of his motion to reopen and he believes an in absentia order of removal was entered prematurely without affording him an opportunity to present any evidence (Br. at 2). These contentions relate to the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reopen-- arguments which more properly should have been advanced in the motion for reconsideration and not on appeal now. To the extent that the respondent also submits affidavits from family and friends and a letter written to his former attorney of record, Ms. Sharon Brown, to establish that he was not properly advised by Ms. Brown, these documents, again, should have been introduced earlier.1 We conclude that the respondent failed to establish a legal or factual basis for reconsideration by the Immigration Judge and specifically, as to the merits of the appeal, we agree with the government that this appeal is not an opportunity for the respondent to file new evidence or advance contentions that should have been made at a prior stage (DHS Br. at 3).
 
*2 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

1

In spite of the respondent’s attorney’s dissatisfaction wtih Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637,639 (BIA 1988), as a matter of law, an ineffective assistance of counsel claim will not lie, if the requirements in Lozada are not met.

IN RE: LUZ MARILU PERDOMO-MERCADO
File: AXXX XX1 731 - E1 Paso, TX
October 22, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Benjamin T. Wiesinger, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lorely R. Fernandez
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The pending motion to reopen, which seeks to reopen and rescind the Immigration Judge’s November 3, 2005, decision ordering the respondent removed in absentia, is both untimely filed and number barred and has not been shown to satisfy any exception to the filing requirements imposed by law on motions to reopen removal proceedings. See section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7).
 
Moreover, we decline to equitably toll the applicable filing deadline based on the respondent’s alleged ineffective assistance of former counsel(s) claim. The respondent contends that former counsel(s) failed to properly pursue her alleged lack of notice of her hearing claim based on her assertion that the address listed on the Notice to Appear does not correctly reflect the address that she provided and, as a result, the notice of hearing was sent to the wrong address. However, the respondent’s claim is undermined by her husband’s affidavit wherein he indicates that the Notice to Appear reflects the address that was provided by the respondent (Submission of Respondent’s Additional Evidence dated December 15, 2011, at 4). Despite the respondent’s assertion that first former counsel forged her signature on the March 3, 2010, dated affidavit that was submitted along with her first motion to reopen that was filed before the Immigration Judge on April 15, 2010, she does not claim that subsequent counsel forged her husband’s signature. Under the circumstances, we do not find the respondent’s assertion alone sufficient to demonstrate that the notice of hearing was sent to the wrong address. The respondent therefore has not sufficiently shown patent error or prejudice. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Nor has she substantially complied with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against each of her former counsels. See also Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (directing Board to apply Lozada standards to ineffective assistance of counsel claims pending the outcome of a rulemaking process).
 
Finally, the respondent, who does not dispute personal service of the Notice to Appear, has not provided a coherent and persuasive explanation for why she waited almost five years to file a motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge. Nor has she demonstrated an exceptional situation that would warrant reopening in the exercise of discretion under the Board’s sua sponte authority.
 
*2 On this record, the respondent has not provided an adequate basis to excuse the fact that her motion is both untimely filed and number barred. Accordingly, we will enter the following order.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

2012 WL 5473615 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ZIYAAD SABAN A.K.A. SABAN ZIYAAD A.K.A. SABAN ZEYAAD
File: AXXX XX3 999 - Miami, FL
October 9, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Leslie Snyder, Esquire
 
CHARGES:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
Lodged: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(i)] - Convicted of crime involving moral turpitude
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of South Africa who adjusted his status to that of a lawful permanent resident on March 16, 1999. On December 3, 2002, the respondent was placed in removal proceedings by the issuance of a Notice to Appear based on his convictions for burglary of an occupied dwelling and grand theft in the third degree under Fla. Stat. §§ 810.02(3)(a) and 812.014(2)(c), respectively. On February 11, 2005, an Immigration Judge entered an order of removal in absentia.
 
On April 19, 2011, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. In the motion, the respondent argued that based on Matter of Alyazji, 25 I&N Dec. 397 (BIA 2011), he was not removable under section 237(a)(2)(A)(i) for a crime involving moral turpitude because he committed the theft and burglary offenses more than 5 years after his 1990 admission. He also argued that he should be permitted to re-enter the United States as a lawful permanent resident because the Notice to Appear was improvidently issued. The Department of Homeland Security opposed the motion to reopen as untimely.
 
On May 17, 2011, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. Relying on 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) and Matter of Armendarez-Mendez, 24 I&N Dec. 646 (BIA 2008), the Immigration Judge concluded that she did not have jurisdiction to reopen the respondent’s in absentia order of removal because the respondent departed the United States. The respondent now appeals. The respondent’s request for oral argument is denied pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7) (2012). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings regarding witness credibility, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i)(2012). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
*2 After the Immigration Judge issued her order denying the respondent’s motion to reopen, the Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit, under whose jurisdiction this case arises, decided Jian Le Lin v. U.S. Atty. Gen., 681 F.3d 1236 (11th Cir. 2012), in which the court disagreed with our holding in Matter of Armendarez-Mendez, supra. The court held that the regulatory departure bar, which prohibits the filing of a motion to reopen by or on behalf of a person who is the subject of removal proceedings subsequent to his or her departure from the United States, conflicts with the statutory right of an alien to file one motion to reopen. Jian Le Lin v. U.S. Atty. Gen., supra, at 1239-41. See also section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(d), 1003.23(b). Thus, under the controlling authority of the Eleventh Circuit, the Immigration Judge had jurisdiction to consider the respondent’s motion to reopen, notwithstanding the respondent’s departure from the United States. Jian Le Lin v. U.S. Atty. Gen., supra. See Matter of Anselmo, 20 I&N Dec. 25 (BIA 1989) (holding that the Board generally must follow circuit court precedent in cases arising in the circuit).
 
Nonetheless, we will affirm the denial of the motion to reopen on alternative grounds. A motion to reopen an in absentia order of removal must be filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances as defined in section 240(e)(1) of the Act. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). A motion to reopen in an absentia removal order pursuant to section 240(b)(5) of the Act may be filed at any time if the alien demonstrates that he did not receive notice of the hearing. Id.
 
The Immigration Judge entered the respondent’s in absentia removal order on February 11, 2005; the respondent filed his motion to reopen on April 19, 2011. The motion to reopen was untimely because the respondent filed it more than 180 days after the entry of the removal order. Further, the respondent does not allege, or establish, that his failure to appear was due to extraordinary circumstances as that term is defined in section 240(e)(1) of the Act. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). He also does not claim that he did not have notice of the February 11, 2005, hearing. Id. Indeed, the respondent’s motion to reopen indicates that his attorney appeared at the February 11, 2005, hearing although the respondent had already departed the United States (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 2; Exh. 4). Based on the record before us, we conclude that the respondent’s motion to reopen his in absentia order of removal was untimely.
 
*3 The respondent states in his motion and on appeal that he relied on erroneous advice of counsel and departed the United States (Respondent’s Br. at 7; Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 2). To the extent that this statement may be construed as a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, we note that the respondent has not complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See Matter of Compean, Bangaly, and J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009).
 
For these reasons we affirm the denial of the motion to reopen, albeit for reasons different from the Immigration Judge. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 5473615 (BIA)
End of Document

2012 WL 5473620 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BARBARA ROBINSON
File: AXXX XX2 823 - Buffalo, NY
October 5, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent has filed an untimely, number-barred motion to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). She seeks equitable tolling due to ineffective assistance of one of her former counsel, Daniel Aidif, who did not file a motion to reopen prior to expiration of the time within which she had to voluntarily depart.
 
The respondent has complied with the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Romero v. United States INS, 399 F.3d 109, 112 (2d Cir. 2005). However, the respondent has not demonstrated a basis for equitable tolling as she has not shown prejudice. Vartelas v. Holder, 620 F.3d 108, 115 (2d Cir. 2010). That is, an alien claiming that she received ineffective assistance of counsel must show that counsel’s “performance was so ineffective as to have impinged upon the fundamental fairness of the hearing.” Debeatham v. Holder, 602 F.3d 481, 485 (2d Cir. 2010)(citations omitted); Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 130 (2d Cir. 2008).
 
The respondent has not made this showing. The Immigration Judge entered the final administrative order in this case on August 5, 2003. The respondent did not enter into a contract with Mr. Aidif until November 17, 2003, which was after expiration of the 90 day period within which to file a timely motion to reopen. Therefore, Mr. Aidif could not have filed a timely motion to reopen or otherwise complied with Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 2002).
 
Further, the respondent has the burden to demonstrate that she exercised due diligence during the “entire” time she seeks to have tolled, which “includes both the period of time before the ineffective assistance of counsel was or should have been discovered and the period from that point until the motion to reopen is filed.” Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 132 (2d Cir. 2008). See also Ali v. Gonzales, 448 F.3d 515, 517 (2d Cir. 2007)(alien must demonstrate that he exercised due diligence in pursuing the case during the period the alien seeks to toll); Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167, 170 (2d Cir. 2006)(“[N]o matter how egregiously ineffective counsel’s assistance may have been, an alien will not be entitled to equitable tolling unless he can affirmatively demonstrate that he exercised reasonable due diligence during the time period sought to be tolled.”).
 
*2 The respondent has not provided a sufficient explanation as to why she waited until 2012 to file the present motion regarding Mr. Aidif. The respondent has filed six previous motions, but she has not specifically alleged ineffective assistance as to Mr. Aidif, or otherwise satisfied the requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, prior to this motion, even though the representation about which she complains occurred in 2003. Therefore, due diligence has not been demonstrated.1
 
Accordingly, the motion to reopen shall be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD

We note that, hand-written on a Form EOIR-27 submitted with the present motion, the respondent also states that her “most recent lawyer, Andrew Rappeport,” did not present the new evidence in connection with a motion filed in 2011. However, no further explanation of this claim has been made, nor is there any evidence of Lozada compliance in connection with any claim against this attorney.
