** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUAN ARMANDO LUA-MOTO
File: AXXX XX2 112 - Chicago, IL
October 2, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gerardo S. Gutierrez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lynn K. Hollander
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 6, 2011. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
Upon de novo review, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent alleged in his motion to reopen that he received ineffective assistance of counsel. However, the respondent did not comply with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) for demonstrating ineffective assistance of counsel or submit sufficient documentation supporting his claim that he is eligible to pursue cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3) (requiring that a motion to reopen be accompanied by the appropriate application for relief and all supporting documents). Furthermore, we find no merit to the respondent’s arguments on appeal, where there is no evidence that a motion for a continuance was denied or that the respondent was ordered removed in absentia. In addition, the respondent has not presented any evidence demonstrating that the Immigration Judge erred in finding that he is inadmissible to the United States. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(c) (placing the burden on the alien to establish that he is clearly and beyond a doubt entitled to be admitted to the United States). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Ana Mann

2012 WL 5473631 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GUILLERMO ANTONIO CORTES FERNANDEZ A.K.A. GUERNO HERNANDEZ CORTEZ
File: AXXX XX0 965 - Los Angeles, CA
October 2, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
James F. Kajtoch, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The final order of removal in this matter was entered on June 3, 2010, when this Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision finding the respondent not eligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). On August 6, 2012, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. The motion will be denied.
 
With certain exceptions, an alien is entitled to file only one motion to reopen and the motion must be filed not later than 90 days after the final administrative order. See section 240(c)(7) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent’s motion is untimely.
 
The respondent asserts, however, that his motion is based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and a claim that equitable tolling applies. A motion to reopen claiming ineffective assistance of counsel must substantially comply with the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640. Prejudice results when the inadequate performance may have affected the outcome of the case. See Mohammed v. Gonzales, 400 F.3d 785 (9th Cir. 2005).
 
The respondent has submitted evidence that after the Board’s decision was issued on June 3, 2010, he entered into an agreement with Michael J. Hernandez for the filing of a petition for review with the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit (Mot. at 1-3; Mot. Exh. A). The respondent has submitted a receipt showing that he paid the amount required for the filing (Mot. Exh. A). The respondent states that Mr. Hernandez advised him that the case would take a long time (Mot. at 2-3). The respondent reports that Mr. Hernandez did not file anything on the respondent’s behalf and that he was suspended from the practice of law by the Florida Bar at the time he entered into the agreement with the respondent to file the petition for review (Mot. at 2-3; Mot. Exh. B). The respondent also states that he now believes the attorney should have filed a motion to reconsider with the Board. The respondent reports that he has obtained the services of a criminal attorney, who specializes in post-conviction relief, to assist him with his criminal conviction, and that once the conviction is addressed, the respondent will be eligible for relief from removal. The respondent has submitted a copy of a visa petition (Form I-130) and evidence that his wife is a United States citizen (Mot. Exh. D).
 
*2 Accepting the respondent’s allegations concerning his former attorney, Michael J. Hernandez, it is extremely unfortunate that the respondent was apparently cheated out of a substantial amount of money. However, the respondent has not made any allegation that the result in his case might have been different if Mr. Hernandez had filed the promised petition for review, or in the alternative filed a motion to reconsider with the Board. It was determined during proceedings that the respondent was not eligible for the relief he sought, based on his controlled substance conviction. The respondent makes no challenge to the findings of the Board or the Immigration Judge. Thus, even though the Board may consider, as a matter of discretion, claims of ineffective assistance based on conduct that occurred after the final order of removal was entered, the respondent has not shown that this is a case that warrants reopening. See Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1, 3 (A.G. 2009).
 
To the extent the respondent speculates that his criminal conviction might be vacated in the future, this is not related to his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and is not a basis for reopening. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 472-73 (BIA 1992) (providing that motions to reopen must establish prima facie eligibility for relief).
 
Finally, the respondent has submitted a supplemental filing with evidence concerning his work-place injury on April 19, 2006. He states that the injury was the subject of a workers compensation claim. He also makes references to a “U Visa” application in relationship to the claim, but he does not explain how any such application would be a basis for reopening or how it would constitute an exception to the 90-day filing requirement.
 
The respondent has not established that his untimely motion to reopen should be granted, and the motion will be denied. The respondent’s request for a stay of removal pending adjudication of the motion is moot.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 5473631 (BIA)
End of Document

2012 WL 5178776 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HISHAM HADI AL MUGOTIR
File: AXXX XX0 436 - Detroit, MI
September 26, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
George P. Mann, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michael B. Dobson
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on December 6, 2011, when we affirmed the decision of the Immigration Judge dated August 12, 2010, which denied the respondent’s motion to reopen the proceedings.1 On January 4, 2012, the respondent filed a motion to reconsider our decision. The motion to reconsider will be granted and the appeal is dismissed. The request for refund of the motion fee is denied. The request for oral argument is denied. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7).
 
We review the findings of fact, including determinations of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether or not the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Iraq, seeks reconsideration by the Board of its December 6, 2011, decision. He alleges we erred in stating he did not submit an appellate brief and, with his motion to reconsider, he has provided a copy of a brief he submitted to the Board concerning his previous appeal. The brief is date stamped indicating that it was received timely by the Board, but it was not associated with the respondent’s record of proceeding. In our previous decision, we stated that no brief had been submitted, but we considered the arguments raised by the respondent previously in his motions to reopen and to reconsider filed with the Immigration Judge (BIA, Dec. 6, 2011, at 2).
 
A motion to reconsider is “a request that the Board reexamine its previous decision in light of additional arguments, a change of law, or perhaps an argument or aspect of the case which was overlooked.” Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56, 57 (BIA 2006). A motion to reconsider challenges the Board’s original decision and alleges that it is defective in some regard. Id. The motion must specify the errors of fact or law in the prior Board decision, and it must be supported by pertinent authority. See section 240(c)(6)(C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(c)(6)(C); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1). Thus, given that the respondent’s appellate brief was overlooked, we grant his motion to reconsider.
 
In his brief, the respondent argues that the previous order of removal entered in absentia should be rescinded. He argues that he has presented exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear as he was ineffectively served by previous counsel and suffered a panic attack concerning his appearance in Immigration Court. He also argues that he is eligible for cancellation of removal and asylum based on changed country conditions in his native Iraq. After considering the arguments raised in the brief, we will again dismiss the appeal.
 
*2 We have reconsidered our December 6, 2011, decision based on the respondent’s appellate brief. We conclude that he has essentially presented the same arguments raised in his previous motions that we considered at the time of our previous decision. As we found previously, he has failed to support his arguments concerning any ineffective assistance of counsel during the earlier proceedings nor has he complied with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); see also Matter of Compean, Bangley & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009). Further, we continue to find that the respondent has failed to show that any anxiety or panic attacks he suffered due to his fear of removal present exceptional circumstances that excuse his failure to appear for his hearing. See Matter of J-P-, 22 I&N Dec. 33 (BIA 1998); see also Scorteanu v. INS, 339 F.3d 407, 411 (6th Cir. 2003) (respondent bears burden of proof to show exceptional circumstances). The respondent’s perfunctory statements concerning his panic attacks, with no detail concerning the severity or evidence of treatment, fail to support his burden of showing exceptional circumstances. See Matter of J-P-, supra. As we are dismissing the appeal from the denial of the motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order and the respondent is currently not in the United States, he has not shown any eligibility for relief and the appeal will be dismissed. Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reconsider is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Michael J. Creppy
FOR THE BOARD

2012 WL 5178781 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DEISY ALVARENGA-RIVERA
File: AXXX XX4 966 - San Antonio, TX
September 25, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jose M. Guerrero, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 15, 2011. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider the denial of her motion to reopen proceedings in which she was ordered removed in absentia. On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred by not finding that her failure to appear was due to exceptional circumstances. The respondent’s appeal will be sustained and the record remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
Upon de novo review, we disagree with the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent did not demonstrate exceptional circumstances for her failure to appear. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(2); 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); Matter of Grijalva-Barrera, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996); see also Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996). According to the evidence in the record, the respondent relied on the advice of her counsel’s office that she did not need to appear for her scheduled hearing. Hence, we find that the respondent received ineffective assistance of counsel, which prevented the respondent from attending her scheduled hearing before the Immigration Judge.
 
Inasmuch as the respondent has demonstrated that she received ineffective assistance of counsel, we will grant the respondent’s motion to reopen and remand the record to the Immigration Judge to provide the respondent with a hearing regarding her removability from the United States and eligibility for relief from removal. Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained.
 
*2 FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing decision.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
Sharon Hoffman
Elise L. Manuel
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 5178781 (BIA)

2012 WL 5178741 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDGAR OROBIO A.K.A. EDGAR OROBIO MENA
File: AXXX XX8 607 - Newark, NJ
September 25, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Maria Ines Gonzalez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 This matter was most recently before us on June 19, 2012, when this Board denied the respondent’s previous untimely motion to reopen his removal proceedings. This motion was filed on July 17, 2012. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent characterizes this motion as a motion to reconsider the Board’s previous decision. A motion to reconsider is one which identifies a material error of fact or law in the decision for which reconsideration is being requested. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1); Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). The respondent’s motion does not identify any errors of fact or law in the prior decision. Instead, the motion seeks to introduce additional evidence in support of the respondent’s prior motion, which sought reopening based on alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. Accordingly, this motion is actually in the style of the respondent’s second, untimely motion to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
The respondent’s prior motion was denied because the untimeliness of the motion was not related to the ineffective assistance, because the respondent had not shown due diligence, because the respondent had not substantially complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and because the respondent had not included the documents which his prior attorney should ostensibly have submitted at the trial level. This motion is accompanied by the respondent’s affidavit and the evidence which he claims should have been submitted to the Immigration Judge. The affidavit generally states that the respondent “contacted [his] attorney at the time afterwards” and was “assured” that “he would have this matter reopened” (Motion, Tab A). He further states that after his prior attorney was “convicted” the respondent “saw many attorneys who told [him] that there was no possibility of re-opening” the case (Id). The respondent’s affidavit does not provide clear details regarding what transpired between the time of his final hearing on April 1, 2005, and the filing of his first motion with the Board on February 8, 2012.
 
These general, vague allegations do not persuade us that equitable tolling of the time and number limits is warranted. Alzaarir v. Att’y Gen., 639 F.3d 86, 90 (3d Cir. 2011). As noted in the Board’s prior decision, the respondent was aware at the trial level that his attorney neglected to submit pertinent documents, and the respondent raised allegations of ineffective assistance through new counsel during the course of his appeal. The respondent has not meaningfully explained why he was unable to make a timely motion to reopen, or comply with Lozada’s procedural requirements during the course of his appeal. His claim that he “saw many attorneys” who refused to file a motion is undercut by his ability to gain new counsel for his appeal who filed a motion to remand which alleged ineffective assistance. We again conclude the respondent has not established that the ineffective assistance is tied to the untimeliness or number-barred nature of this motion, and that the respondent has not established due diligence. This motion to reopen will be denied as untimely and number-barred.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 5178741 (BIA)

2012 WL 5178750 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NICHOLAS DONALD DALE RUSSELL
File: AXXX XX1 224 - Las Vegas, NV
September 21, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Douglas Jalaie, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board in 2005 when we summarily affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision denying a previous motion to reopen these proceedings, in which the respondent was ordered removed in absentia in 2001. The respondent has filed another untimely motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion, which will be denied.
 
A motion to reopen seeks a new hearing based on new or previously unavailable evidence that is material to the respondent’s claims. Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56, 57-58 (BIA 2006); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). Because the respondent has not submitted evidence with the motion, it will be denied for not complying with the regulatory requirements for motions to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). Even if we considered the merits of the motion, it would be denied, as a motion to reopen is not an opportunity to repeat previously rejected arguments. See INS v. Wang, 450 U.S. 139, 141 (1981) (discussing motions to reopen); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, 402 (BIA 1991). Nor has the respondent meaningfully raised ineffective assistance of counsel, where he has not complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), or shown prejudice.
 
Further, the respondent has not demonstrated an exceptional situation warranting the exercise of our discretion to reopen the proceedings sua sponte. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The request for a stay is denied as moot.
 
David B. Holmes

2012 WL 5178753 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SASENARINE SINGH
File: AXXX XX9 499 - New York, NY
September 20, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lena Golovnin
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This is the respondent’s fifth motion before the Board and it will be denied as untimely and number barred. This case was last before the Board on February 29, 2012, when we denied the respondent’s untimely and number barred motion to reopen these removal proceedings. The final administrative order in these proceedings was entered on July 22, 2003, almost a decade ago. With certain exceptions, a motion to reopen must be filed within 90 days of the administratively final order, and only one may be filed. Sections 240(c)(7)(A) and (C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(7)(A) & (C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The record reflects, however, that the Board did not receive the motion until June 15, 2012, and its supplemental filings on July 9 and September 12, 2012. The motion to reopen and its supplements are filed years out of time and are number-barred. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion.
 
The respondent claims that he received ineffective assistance of counsel at the time his appeal was dismissed by the Board in 2003. He asserts counsel failed to file a timely petition for review of our decision or a timely motion to reopen these proceedings. The respondent therefore claims he is entitled to equitable tolling of the filing deadline for a motion to reopen. However, even if the respondent has complied with the procedural requirements to establish ineffective assistance of counsel as required in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), he has not demonstrated due diligence in his filing of the instant motion or his prior motions. See Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 132 (2d Cir. 2008); see also Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 135 (2d Cir. 2000). The respondent has not explained his actions in waiting more than 9 years after the issuance of the final order, or even 3 years after his first untimely motion to reopen was denied by this Board, to file the ineffective assistance claim. Moreover, the respondent has not shown how he was prejudiced by prior counsel’s actions. The respondent has not established that he was not removable as charged, that he would be eligible to pursue adjustment of status in these proceedings, or that he did not have a fair hearing on his application for asylum, withholding of removal or protection under the Convention Against Torture. In short, he has not shown that the outcome of these proceedings would be different but for the actions of prior counsel.
 
*2 The respondent also has not presented any evidence of changed country conditions in order to except him from the time and number restrictions in filing his motion to reopen.
 
Finally, the respondent has not demonstrated an exceptional situation that warrants our reopening sua sponte. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
Accordingly, the motion to reopen will be denied as untimely and number-barred.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 5178753 (BIA)
End of Document

2012 WL 5178758 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OSWAIN BRENTNOL WALCOTT
File: AXXX XX2 779 - Lumpkin, GA
September 19, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 This case was previously before the Board when we returned the record to the Immigration Court, because we found that the respondent waived appeal through counsel. Thus, the Immigration Judge’s decision ordering the respondent removed to Guyana became the final administrative order. The respondent has filed a motion to reconsider our decision and reopen proceedings.1 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion, which will be denied.
 
A party seeking reconsideration requests that the Board’s original decision be reexamined in light of alleged legal or factual errors, a change of law, or an argument that was overlooked. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). The respondent has not identified errors of fact or law in our prior decision. Instead, the respondent repeats the arguments he previously made to the Board. Compare Respondent’s Motion with Respondent’s Notice of Appeal, attached statement. We considered these arguments and found them unpersuasive before concluding that the respondent waived appeal through counsel. See Matter of O-S-G-, supra, at 58 (stating that “a motion to reconsider is not a process by which a party may submit, in essence, the same brief presented on appeal ....”). As we explained, ineffective assistance is not clear from the record, and the respondent did not comply with the procedural requirements for raising such a claim. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). Nor, for reasons set forth, did his filing reflect circumstances that warranted review of the Immigration Judge’s decision by certification. Thus, while the respondent may disagree with our decision, he has presented no arguments that convince us of a factual or legal error, or an appellate argument that we overlooked.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen will also be denied. Notwithstanding the respondent’s recent partial compliance with Matter of Lozada - filing a complaint with prior counsel’s state bar - the respondent has still not shown that counsel’s alleged deficient performance resulted in prejudice. See Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1274 (11th Cir. 2005) (concluding that an alien must show prejudice when he advances an ineffective assistance of counsel claim and that “[p]rejudice exists when the performance of counsel is so inadequate that there is a reasonable probability that but for the attorney’s error, the outcome of the proceedings would have been different”). Neither the respondent’s present or previous filing included proffered evidence reflecting prima facie evidence of United States citizenship. Additionally, the respondent has presented no evidence that his criminal conviction underlying these proceedings has been overturned or that a state court has otherwise found ineffective assistance by the respondent’s defense counsel of the type in Padilla v. Kentucky, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010). Finally, the respondent has not alleged or demonstrated prima facie eligibility for relief from removal.
 
*2 The respondent has submitted a photocopy of a portion of a detention center computer-based mailing log and submits that his notice of appeal would have been timely but for unspecified administrative errors within the detention center’s mailing system. However, even if the respondent had timely filed the Notice of Appeal, it would not impact our conclusion that he waived appeal through counsel. A respondent who waives appeal cannot thereafter file an appeal. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a)(1) (final sentence), 1003.39.
 
Finally, we do not find an adequate basis for reconsidering our previous decision or reopening the case sua sponte. Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We will treat the motion to reconsider as timely.


2012 WL 5178758 (BIA)

2012 WL 5178809 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: STEPHEN GIKERA NJOGU
File: AXXX XX6 041 - Dallas, TX
September 6, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
N. Jude Menes, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Cynthia D. Goodman
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)] - In the United States in violation of law
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s December 22, 2010 decision denying his motion to reopen. The respondent filed his motion with the Immigration Judge on December 2, 2010 while claiming that he received ineffective assistance of counsel at the time of the Immigration Judge’s August 5, 2010 pre-conclusion voluntary departure grant under section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a). The respondent’s request for oral argument is denied. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We hold that the Immigration Judge correctly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. In particular, while the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge improperly focused on the untimely nature of his motion in light of his prior counsel’s ineffective assistance (i.e., which may toll the filing deadline for his motion), we find that his claim for reopening should be denied as he failed to show any prejudice given that he is not currently eligible for any form of relief (Department of Homeland Security’s (DHS) Br. at 1). See INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988) (an applicant seeking reopening must establish prima facie eligibility for the underlying relief sought).
 
The respondent maintains that his former counsel failed to appear in court for his master calendar hearing--which allegedly led to the respondent accepting voluntary departure despite lacking full understanding of its implications--and that such counsel falsely promised various forms of immigration relief, actions which ultimately deprived the respondent of “an opportunity to present the bona fides of his valid and legitimate marriage to a U.S. citizen” (Respondent’s Br. at 4-6, 10-11). Even accepting the respondent’s contention that he substantively complied with the procedural requirements described in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), for demonstrating ineffective assistance of counsel, he has not established prejudice from the actions of his prior counsel as he has not provided any evidence showing that the outcome of his case might have been different if such counsel acted with sufficient competence. See U.S. v. Herrera, 412 F.3d 577, 580 (5th Cir. 2005) (noting that in order to prove prejudice, an applicant must show that there is a reasonable probability that, but for counsel’s unprofessional errors, the result of the proceeding would have been different) (internal citations omitted). In particular, we are not persuaded by the respondent’s argument that had his counsel been effective, the respondent would have presented evidence demonstrating that his marriage was legitimate as he has not addressed, much less reconciled, the fact that the DHS previously issued an intent to deny his wife’s petition for alien relative (Form I-130) that was filed on his behalf upon concluding that their marriage was not bona fide and that it was entered into for the purpose of evading immigration laws (Exh. 2; Respondent’s Br. at 8, 11). See, e.g., Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009); Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 2002). While the respondent further states that he and his wife remain married and that another I-130 petition that was filed on his behalf is pending with the DHS, we are not inclined to remand the matter for additional proceedings to await adjudication of such a petition based on the particular facts of this case (Respondent’s Br. at 12-13). See generally Morgan v. Gonzales, 445 F.3d 549, 552 (2d Cir. 2006) (finding no abuse of discretion in the denial of a continuance for adjudication of a second visa petition when the first I-130 filed by the same petitioner was denied by the USCIS for failure to establish a bona fide marriage).
 
*2 To the extent the respondent contends that his former counsel misrepresented his eligibility for asylum, the respondent has not presented any intelligible argument suggesting that he may be able to demonstrate eligibility for asylum or any other form of relief (Respondent’s Br. at 8). See, e.g., Matter of Almanza, 24 I&N Dec. 771 (BIA 2009) (the burden is on the respondent to establish eligibility for relief). As we see no evidence before us indicating that a remand of the proceedings might lead to a different result in this matter, the respondent’s charges of ineffective assistance of counsel do not undermine the Immigration Judge’s finding of removability or underlying decision.
 
Further, while the respondent has filed supplemental evidence on appeal, we may not accept these documents into the record as we are precluded from engaging in fact-finding in the course of deciding appeals. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003-1(d)(3)(iv). Even considering such information, the respondent has not identified any specific evidence which, if presented, would have affected the proceeding below, nor has he illustrated how the Immigration Judge’s decision remains incomplete or defective. As such, the respondent has not established that a remand is warranted for any reason. See Rodriguez-Carrillo, 22 I&N Dec. 1031 (BIA 1999). We also find that the circumstances presented by the respondent are not “exceptional” as to justify a sua sponte reopening of the matter (Respondent’s Br. at 11-12). See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). To the extent that the respondent seeks a stay of removal, he has not shown why the execution of his removal order should be stayed and we accordingly deny his request in this regard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(f). Accordingly, based on the foregoing, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 5178809 (BIA)

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JIANLING LIAN
File: AXXX XX7 991 - Baltimore, MD
August 30, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
K. Thomas Li, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Carrie E. Johnston
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of China, was ordered removed in absentia on March 8, 2004. On January 15, 2009, the respondent filed a motion to reopen proceedings, which the Immigration Judge denied on June 26, 2009. The respondent filed a timely appeal of that decision. The appeal will be sustained, proceedings will be reopened and the record will be remanded.
 
The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen finding that his motion was untimely and did not warrant sua sponte reopening. We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), are satisfied.
 
Upon review, we find that in light of the circumstances presented in this case, including evidence reflecting that the respondent’s former counsel provided ineffective assistance with respect to the respondent’s application for adjustment of status which resulted in the issuance of the Notice to Appear and the respondent being placed into proceedings as well as his direction to the respondent not to appear at his March 8, 2004, hearing, we will allow the respondent another opportunity to appear for a hearing. We note that the respondent acted diligently upon discovering his former counsel’s deficient performance. In reaching this conclusion, we further note that he respondent is the beneficiary of an approved I-130.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained, proceedings are reopened and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
Sharon C Hoffman
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 3911874 (BIA)

2012 WL 3911846 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDGAR ISRAEL MARTINEZ
File: AXXX XX3 185 - Los Angeles, CA
August 24, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On August 21, 2009, the respondent failed to appear for his removal hearing and was ordered removed in absentia by the Immigration Judge. The respondent subsequently filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge, which was denied in a November 3, 2010, decision. The respondent appealed the Immigration Judge’s denial, but the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal in a June 28, 2011, decision. On July 26, 2012, the respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Board based on the alleged ineffectiveness of his former counsel. For the reasons set out below, the respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied.
 
As an initial matter, the respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely and number-barred and will be denied. The final order in these proceedings was entered by the Board on June 28, 2011. Generally, a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision, and only one may be filed. Sections 240(c)(7)(A) and (C)(i)ofthe Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(7)(A) & (C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). A request for rescission of an in absentia order of removal based on “exceptional circumstances” must be filed within 180 days after the date that the order of removal is issued. See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(3) and 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). The present motion was filed on July 26, 2012, well beyond the filing deadlines. In addition, the respondent previously filed a motion to reopen that was denied by the Immigration Judge on November 3, 2010.
 
However, aliens raising an ineffective assistance of counsel claim in a motion can have the time limits on motions equitably tolled. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003) (holding that equitable tolling will be applied where the alien is prevented from timely filing a motion by deception, fraud, or error so long as the alien acted with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error). Aliens relying on equitable tolling have the burden of demonstrating that they exercised due diligence in discovering the fraud or error.
 
We find that the respondent’s untimely and number-barred motion to reopen does not qualify for equitable tolling. The respondent has not demonstrated that he exercised the requisite diligence in filing the instant motion. The respondent was ordered removed in absentia on August 21, 2009, and on June 28, 2011, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s November 3, 2010, decision denying his motion to reopen. However, the respondent waited until July 26, 2012, to file the instant motion, and has not specified what he did between June 28 2011, and July 26, 2012, to protect his rights or when he definitely learned of his prior counsel’s incompetence. Given the lack of evidence regarding the steps he took to preserve his rights from June 2011 to July 2012, we find that the respondent did not act with due diligence. See Singh v. Gonzales, 491 F.3d 1090, 1096 (9th Cir. 2007) (refusing to toll where immigrant failed to investigate suspicions of incompetence for 6 months). In any event, the respondent has not submitted the requisite application for relief. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1).
 
*2 We alternatively find that the respondent has not substantially complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).1 See generally, Apolinar v. Mukasey, 514 F.3d 893 (9th Cir. 2008). The respondent has not included a personal affidavit or any correspondence to his prior counsel regarding his allegations of ineffectiveness. See Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592, 598-99 (9th Cir. 2004) (finding that alien did not comply with Lozada where he did not submit an affidavit and did not provide former counsel with an adequate opportunity to respond to allegation). The respondent also has not filed a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority nor adequately explained why he has not done so.
 
Finally, the respondent has not shown that an “exceptional situation” exists that would warrant the Board’s exercise of its discretion to reopen these proceedings sua sponte. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (stating that “as a general matter, we invoke our sua sponte authority sparingly, treating it not as a general remedy for any hardships created by enforcement of time and number limits in the motions regulations, but as an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations”). Accordingly, the following orders are entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s request for a stay is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Under Matter of Lozada, an ineffective assistance of counsel claim must satisfy the following procedural requirements: (1) an affidavit of the alien attesting to the relevant facts, including a detailed description of the agreement with former counsel, (2) former counsel must be informed of the allegations and provided with an opportunity to respond, and (3) indicate whether a complaint has been filed the appropriate disciplinary authority, and if not, why not. Lozada, supra.

MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Shan D. Potts, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On March 22, 2012, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision denying his motion to terminate these removal proceedings. On May 9, 2012, the respondent filed the instant timely motion to reopen to enable him to apply for adjustment of status based on an approved immediate relative visa petition, dated October 30, 2006, classifying him as the parent of a United States citizen. See section 240(c)(7) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The Department of Homeland Security has not filed a statement opposing this motion. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(g). The motion will be granted.
 
The respondent, who was previously represented by the above-named attorney before the Immigration Judge and the Board, now claims that he did not pursue an application for adjustment of status previously in these proceedings due to ineffective assistance of counsel. We find that ineffective assistance of counsel in handling the respondent’s potential eligibility for adjustment is sufficiently clear on the face of the record that reopening is warranted even without full compliance with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See Rodriguez-Lariz v. I.N.S., 282 F.3d 1218, 1227 (9th Cir. 2002). Accordingly, the respondent’s timely, unopposed motion to reopen will be granted, and the record remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this decision.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this decision.
 
Molly Kendall-Clark

2012 WL 3911658 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GIOVANNI JOSE MARTINO MORENO A.K.A. GIOVANNI MARTINO A.K.A. GIOVANNI J. MARTINO A.K.A. JOHNATHON MARTINO A.K.A. GIOVANNI J. GHOST A.K.A. GIOVANNI MARTIN A.K.A. BLOODY G. MARTINO A.K.A. MARTINO GIOVANNI
File: AXXX XX6 466 - New York, NY
August 8, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; reconsideration
 
*1 The Board entered the final administrative order of removal in the respondent’s case on July 28, 2011, and on June 27, 2012, the respondent filed the instant motion with the Board. Whether construed as a motion to reopen or as a motion to reconsider, the respondent’s motion is untimely. See Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991) (discussing motions to reopen and motions to reconsider); sections 240(c)(6)(B), (C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(6)(B), (C)(i); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(b)(2), (c)(2). The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent has raised a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel in the instant motion. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988) (setting forth the procedural requirements for ineffective assistance of counsel claims before the Board). He specifically claims that his former attorney did not properly contest on appeal the Immigration Judge’s decision finding him removable as charged and ineligible for relief; did not file a timely motion to reopen the proceedings; and did not file a timely petition for judicial review of the Board’s prior order.
 
The filing deadline imposed on motions to reopen may be equitably tolled where the motion is based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Jin Bo Zhao v. I.N.S., 452 F.3d 154, 160 (2nd Cir. 2006). In order to prevail on his ineffective assistance of counsel claim, however, the respondent must substantially comply with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra. See Jian Yun Zheng v. U.S. Dep’t of Justice, 409 F.3d 43, 47 (2nd Cir. 2005). (“[A]n alien who has failed to comply substantially with the Lozada requirements in her motion to reopen before the BIA forfeits her ineffective assistance of counsel claim”).
 
Before allegations of ineffective assistance of former counsel are presented to the Board, former counsel must be informed of the allegations and allowed the opportunity to respond. Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639. In the present case, the respondent has failed to comply with Lozada’s notice requirement. There is no evidence now before the Board to show that the respondent has made any attempt to inform his former attorney of the allegations against her, or that his former attorney has otherwise been made aware of the allegations. Further, the respondent has not explained why he has not properly notified his former attorney of the allegations against her. Id.
 
*2 In certain circumstances, “slavish adherence” to the requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, is unnecessary. See Ruiz-Martinez v. Mukasey, 516 F.3d 102, 121 (2d Cir.2008). In the respondent’s case, however, ineffective assistance of counsel is not apparent on the face of the record. See YiLong Yang v. Gonzales, 478 F.3d 133, 143 (2nd Cir.2007). Under the circumstances, we find that the respondent has failed to substantially comply with the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim before the Board. See Jian Yun Zheng v. U.S. Dep’t of Justice, supra.
 
Inasmuch as the respondent has not set forth a viable ineffective assistance of counsel claim, equitable tolling of the filing deadlines is not warranted in this case. Further, no exception to the filing deadline imposed on motions to reopen appears to apply to this motion, and no exceptional situation has been shown to be present in this case to warrant the exercise of the Board’s limited authority to reopen or reconsider cases sua sponte. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(a), (c)(3). Accordingly, the respondent’s untimely motion to reopen and reconsider will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen and reconsider is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 3911658 (BIA)
End of Document

2012 WL 3276580 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ELISEO PEREYRA
File: AXXX XX4 954 - Cleveland, OH
July 25, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Margaret W. Wong, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michael A. Tripi
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 29, 2011. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
Upon de novo review, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen removal proceedings. We find that the respondent’s contention that his in absentia order of removal should be vacated because he had departed the United States prior to his removal hearing is without merit. In Matter of Brown, 18 I&N Dec. 324, 325 (BIA 1982), the Board held that an alien “cannot compel the termination of deportation proceedings which have been commenced against him merely by effecting a departure and reentry.” Once jurisdiction was vested with the Immigration Judge by the filing of the Notice to Appear (Form I-862), the Immigration Judge obtained jurisdiction and the respondent remained subject to removal proceedings, even though he departed the United States and re-entered. Hence, when the respondent did not appear for his scheduled removal hearing, we find that the Immigration Judge properly entered an order of removal.1
 
To the extent that the respondent argues that his failure to appear for his hearing was due to ineffective assistance of counsel, we find that the respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely. See 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C) (requiring that motion to reopen based on exceptional circumstances be filed within 180 days of the date of the order of removal). Moreover, the respondent has not fully complied with the procedural requirements for demonstrating ineffective assistance of counsel as set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).2
 
Furthermore, we find that the respondent has not established that his motion should be granted pursuant to our sua sponte authority. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (holding that the Board’s power to reopen or reconsider cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional situations and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship). The respondent has not demonstrated an exceptional situation sufficient to warrant reopening of his removal proceedings. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Ana Mann
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: JOSE JESUS ESTRADA-GARCIA A.K.A. JOSE GARCIA ESTRADA
File: AXXX XX9 680 - San Diego, CA
July 20, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Francisco Javier Aldana, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Ted Y. Yamada
Deputy Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, was ordered removed in absentia on June 9, 2011. On June 13, 2011, the respondent filed a motion to reopen proceedings, which the Immigration Judge denied on August 15, 2011. The respondent filed a timely appeal of that decision. The appeal will be sustained, proceedings will be reopened and the record will be remanded.
 
The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen finding that he had received proper notice for his June 9, 2011, hearing. We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), are satisfied.
 
Upon review, we find that in view of the above and in light of the totality of circumstances presented in this case, including evidence reflecting that the respondent’s counsel conceded that he provided ineffective assistance by failing to appear at the June 9, 2011, hearing, we will allow the respondent another opportunity to appear for a hearing.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained, proceedings are reopened and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
Sharon C Hoffman

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

IN RE: ANEL TAPIA

July 16, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
William Pryor, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Angela Marra
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 This case has been the subject of two prior Board decisions. In the Board’s decision on September 21, 2011, the appeal was dismissed, and a motion to remand based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel was denied. In denying the motion to remand, the Board found that there had been no compliance with the threshold procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, noting that more than one year had elapsed since the appeal was filed. On March 9, 2012, the Board denied a motion for reconsideration of the September 21, 2011, decision and a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance before the Immigration Judge, finding that the respondent and current counsel could have pursued an ineffective assistance of counsel claim with Lozada compliance before the Immigration Judge or on appeal. See Tr. at 12-13. The respondent has filed a motion to reconsider the Board’s decisions on March 9, 2012, and September 21, 2011. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the motion, which will be denied.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2), only one motion to reconsider a Board decision is permitted, and a party may not seek reconsideration of a decision denying a previous motion to reconsider. Consequently, the respondent’s second motion for reconsideration of the September 21, 2011, decision, and motion for reconsideration of the March 9, 2012, denial of reconsideration will be denied. The respondent also has not shown that reconsideration of the March 2012 denial of reopening is warranted, as there is no legal or factual defect in the decision denying reopening. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1); Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). The respondent’s tactical decision to delay pursuit of an ineffective assistance of counsel claim and/or compliance with the procedural threshold requirements for such a claim is not tantamount to showing that evidence in support of such a claim could not have been obtained and presented at the hearing below. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c).
 
ORDER: The motion for reconsideration is denied.
 
Molly Kendall Clark

2012 WL 3276552 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DESIREE ANN INGRAM A.K.A. DESIREE ANN DAKERS
File: AXXX XX2 242 - Los Angeles, CA
July 6, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The final administrative order of removal in these proceedings was entered by the Board on August 31, 2006. On April 27, 2012, alleging ineffective of former counsels, the respondent, a native and citizen of Belize, filed the pending motion to reopen. However, the motion is untimely filed and has not been shown to qualify for any exception to the filing requirements imposed on motions to reopen removal proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). Moreover, we decline to equitably toll the applicable filing deadlines. The respondent does not specifically contend that she was unaware of the Board’s August 31, 2006, decision. Nor does she specially assert or indicate in any way that the alleged action or inaction of former counsels prevented her from timely filing the instant motion. Indeed, she does not dispute that she was aware of the alleged deficient performances of former counsels prior to the Board’s August 31, 2006, decision. Under the circumstances, particularly where, as here, first former counsel describes the respondent’s lack of cooperation in pursuing her claim, we do not find that the respondent has sufficiently demonstrated that she received ineffective assistance. Nor has she sufficiently complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against each of her former counsels, including the requirement that she show prejudice as a result of the alleged ineffective assistance. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, supra; Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003). See also Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009). Nor has there been any showing of due diligence in pursuing an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See Iturribarria v. INS, supra.
 
On this record, the respondent has not provided us with an adequate reason to excuse the untimeliness of her motion. Nor has she demonstrated any exceptional circumstance that would warrant the reopening of these proceedings under the Board’s discretionary sua sponte authority. Accordingly, the respondent’s motion and related request for a stay of removal are denied.1
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

However, the respondent’s appellate fee waiver request is granted. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.8(a)(3).

2012 WL 2834968 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE CABRERA-LEON
File: AXXX XX5 507 - New York, NY
June 25, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Bibiana C. Andrade, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Angela Marra
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On October 31, 2011, the Board entered the final administrative order in this case, and on February 29, 2012, the Board denied the respondent’s previous motion to reopen. On March 29, 2012, the respondent filed the instant untimely, number-barred motion to reopen. See sections 240(c)(7)(A), (C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(7)(A), (C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent now raises an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). He alleges prejudicial error on the part of his former attorney because she entered pleading on his behalf, thereby admitting to his being removable under section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i) (present without being admitted or paroled), thus precluding his eligibility for adjustment of status. He specifically argues that his former attorney should have denied the allegation that the respondent is an alien present in the United States without being admitted and paroled because he was paroled into the United States pursuant to a grant of advanced parole under section 212(d)(5)(A) of the Act.
 
It is undisputed that the respondent first entered the United States without inspection on or about March 15, 1999, as alleged in the Notice to Appear, and that he was subsequently authorized to be paroled upon his return to the United States to pursue a pending application for adjustment of status. The fact that the respondent was paroled into the United States pursuant to a grant of advanced parole does not, under the circumstances, render the respondent admissible. See Ibragimov v. Gonzales, 476 F.3d 125 (2nd Cir. 2007); Matter of G-A-C-, 22 I&N Dec. 83, 88-89 (BIA 1998) (explaining that section 212(d)(5) of the Act makes clear that the parole shall not be regarded as an admission of the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 212.5(e)(2)(i). In the present case, the respondent was properly placed in removal proceedings and charged with inadmissibility following the denial by the DHS of his application for adjustment of status on August 8, 2003, and the revocation of his parole.
 
For the foregoing reasons, the respondent has not established that he suffered prejudice as a result of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Rabiu v. I.N.S., 41 F.3d 879, 882-83 (2nd Cir. 1994); Matter of Lozada, supra, at 640. Therefore, equitable tolling of the filing restrictions imposed on this motion is not warranted. See Iavorski v. U.S. I.N.S., 232 F.3d 124 (2nd Cir. 2000). Further, it does not appear that any exception to the filing restrictions applies to this motion or that any exceptional situation is present in this case to warrant the exercise of the Board’s sua sponte authority. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(a), (c)(3). Accordingly, the respondent’s untimely, number-barred motion to reopen will be denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Molly Kendall-Clark
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 2834968 (BIA)
End of Document

2012 WL 2835215 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDGAR OROBIO
File: AXXX XX8 607 - Newark, NJ
June 19, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Maria Ines Gonzalez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Patricia M. Medeiros
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before us on July 20, 2006, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal and denied his request for a remand based on allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel. This motion, filed on February 8, 2012, is not timely. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security opposes reopening. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent argues that he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. We construe this as an argument that the time limits on motions should be equitably tolled. The time limits on motions may be equitably tolled due to ineffective assistance where an alien establishes that he was prejudiced by the misconduct, and where he has shown due diligence in discovering and correcting the errors. See Alzaarir v. Att’y Gen., 639 F.3d 86, 90 (3d Cir. 2011).
 
We are not persuaded that equitable tolling is warranted in this case.
 
First, the purported ineffective assistance all occurred at the trial level, and as the respondent knew of the ostensible failures of his prior counsel at that time the untimeliness of this motion is not related to the ineffective assistance. Accordingly, equitable tolling does not apply.
 
Second, the respondent has not shown due diligence in this case. He previously raised these arguments of ineffective assistance during the course of his appeal 6 years ago and requested a remand. We denied the respondent’s request for a remand because he failed to submit the necessary corroborative evidence. The respondent has not provided any explanation for why he waited over 5 years to submit another motion.
 
The respondent’s motion does not substantially comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). He has not provided evidence of his agreement with his former attorney, he has not indicated whether he has submitted a bar complaint, and there is no evidence that the former attorney has been notified of these allegations. The respondent’s arguments relating to ineffective assistance rest primarily on his former attorney’s suspension, seemingly in an unrelated matter, which does not establish that there was ineffective assistance in this case.
 
Finally, the respondent has not included the documents which his prior attorney should ostensibly have obtained and submitted. Without being able to examine the proffered evidence, we are unable to conclude that the respondent was prejudiced in any respect.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 2835215 (BIA)
End of Document

2012 WL 2835217 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SHIRLAND MARCIA MCDONALD
File: AXXX XX7 840 - New York, NY
June 18, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Genet Getachew, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision issued June 27, 2011, denying her request to reopen proceedings after she was ordered removed in absentia.1 The Department of Homeland Security has not filed a brief or other response. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including credibility findings, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). The Board reviews de novo all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges, including legal and discretionary determinations and applications of law to fact. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent did not demonstrate exceptional circumstances for her failure to appear at the February 8, 2011, hearing. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i). The respondent on appeal has not challenged the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent did not comply with the requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See generally Matter of Grijalva-Barrera, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996) (finding that ineffective assistance of counsel may amount to “exceptional circumstances” in the context of a motion to reopen an in absentia removal order).2 The respondent has not identified the individual whom she claims purported to be able to assist her, or whether or not the individual was an attorney or otherwise authorized to represent her under section 8 C.F.R. § 1292.1. The record does not contain a Form EOIR-28, Notice of Entry of Appearance as Attorney or Representative before the Immigration Court, or other evidence of representation or agreement with the respondent. To the extent the respondent’s attorney on appeal makes general assertions about the individual and the respondent’s experiences, statements of counsel are not evidence. See, e.g., INS v. Phinpathya, 464 U.S. 183, 188-89 n.6 (1984); Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980).
 
In the event that the individual was a non-attorney, the extent to which the Matter of Lozada requirements are mandated is an unsettled question, but some compliance has been required, generally of the first two steps. See Omar v. Mukasey, 517 F.3d 647, 651 (2d Cir. 2008); Matter of Zmijewska, 24 I&N Dec. 87, 94-95 (BIA 2007) (finding that the respondent satisfied the concerns underlying Matter of Lozada where she submitted to the Board a copy of a formal complaint provided to her accredited representative, whose representation and specific action had been described to the Board, and apparently offered the accredited representative an opportunity to respond). In contrast, the respondent in the instant matter has not identified the individual or the capacity in which he or she presented his or her ability to assist the respondent, the nature of the agreement between the individual and the respondent, any proof of payment, what specific representations the individual made to the respondent about her immigration proceedings, or what the individual said, if anything, specifically about the February 8, 2011, hearing at which the respondent was ordered removed in absentia. The respondent has also not provided evidence that a formal complaint was provided to the individual with an opportunity to respond.
 
*2 Furthermore, the respondent has not established that she was prejudiced by the individual’s actions. See Omar v. Mukasey, supra, at 51 (finding no error where the Board determined that the respondent had presented neither sufficient compliance with Matter of Lozada, supra, to whatever extent it may be required for non-attorneys, nor prejudice from the alleged actions); see also Matter of Assaad, supra, at 561-62. The respondent has not demonstrated that she would have attended the February 8, 2011, hearing but for the advice of the individual. We note that the respondent, to whom the Notice to Appear with the time, date, and location of the Immigration Court hearing was mailed, has not argued that she did not have notice of the hearing (Exh. 1). The respondent’s statement provided with the motion to reopen does not indicate that she was advised to not attend the hearing. Moreover, the respondent has not contested the ground of removability under section 237(a)(1)(D)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(D)(i), nor demonstrated eligibility for any form of relief, including voluntary departure which she mentioned briefly in the motion to reopen. We therefore conclude that the respondent has not shown prejudice based upon the alleged ineffectiveness of the unidentified individual.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Elise L. Manuel
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Counsel for the respondent has not filed a separate written brief or statement, despite indicating on the Notice of Appeal that a brief would be submitted and despite seeking and receiving a briefing extension. We considered the statements on the Notice of Appeal as the basis of the respondent’s appeal, although they lack specificity. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(2)(A), (E) (permitting summary dismissal in express situations, including failure to file a brief as indicated).

2

Under Matter of Lozada, supra, a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must be supported by (1) an affidavit setting out the agreement entered into with counsel, (2) proof that notice of the allegations and an opportunity to respond has been provided to counsel whose representation was allegedly ineffective, and (3) the filing of a complaint with appropriate disciplinary authorities or an explanation for the failure to file a complaint. See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 561-62 (BIA 2003) (requiring prejudice stemming from prior counsel’s actions in addition to compliance with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra).


2012 WL 2835217 (BIA)
