2012 WL 2835224 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: VICTOR MANUEL RAMIREZ-RAMIREZ
File: AXXX XX0 382 - Los Angeles, CA
June 14, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Vera A. Weisz, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Remand
 
In a decision dated May 10, 2011, an Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed to Mexico after determining that he had not been cooperating with his attorney and was not eligible for relief other than voluntary departure; the Immigration Judge denied voluntary departure as a matter of discretion. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal, the respondent alleges that his prior attorney who represented him before the Immigration Judge failed to explore all of the respondent’s immigration remedies and poorly advised him, including telling him to inform the Immigration Judge that he needed more time because he was getting married, when in reality he was not (Respondent’s Br. at 1, 3, 6-8). He argues that his former attorney did not raise the issue of the respondent qualifying for a U visa and his counselor withdrew from the case when the respondent refused to make a false claim of political asylum (Respondent’s Br. at 6, 8).
 
The respondent does not claim to have met the procedural requirements for establishing a prima facie case of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (stating that in order to prevail on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must establish and include the following: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not); see also Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097, 1100 (9th Cir. 1999) (stating that the Lozada requirements “insures that the record contains the ‘essential information’ necessary to ‘evaluate the substance’ of the alien’s claim”).
 
While the respondent is correct that strict compliance with Lozada’s procedural requirements for establishing a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is not required in the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, particularly where the record reflects a “clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance of counsel” (Respondent’s Br. at 6), we do not find this to be the case here. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000). The fact that the respondent did not comply with the Lozada requirements precludes any presumption that the respondent was provided with ineffective assistance of counsel. Additionally, although the respondent alleges that his prior counsel told him to lie to the Immigration Judge about getting married and advised him to file a false asylum application, there is no proof that his prior counsel was actively engaging in fraud. With such allegations, it would be particularly helpful if the respondent did notify his counsel of these allegations and submitted a response from the attorney in order to facilitate the evaluation of such conduct.
 
*2 Nevertheless, there is no evidence that the respondent was prejudiced by his former attorney’s actions such that he was prevented from reasonably presenting his case or from applying for relief which would have been available to him. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 899 (9th Cir. 2003) (stating that ineffective assistance of counsel results in a denial of due process under the Fifth Amendment only when the proceeding is so fundamentally unfair that the alien is prevented from reasonable presenting his case); see also Rodriguez-Gonzalez v. INS, 640 F.2d 1139, 1142 (9th Cir. 1981) (stating that legal strategies which are unwise in hindsight do not constitute ineffective assistance of counsel). The respondent has not established that the proceedings would have been different but for his counsel’s alleged unprofessional errors. The respondent’s prior counsel appears to have asked to withdraw from representing the respondent during a preliminary hearing but was asked to remain of counsel to give the respondent another opportunity to file applications for relief (Tr. at 17-19). At the next hearing, the respondent still had not presented any applications, after being explicitly informed by the Immigration Judge that this was his last continuance for filing applications (Tr. at 20-21), and his attorney again alleged a lack of cooperation as grounds for seeking withdrawal (Tr. at 22). The Immigration Judge then found the respondent’s applications abandoned and granted the respondent’s counsel’s request to withdraw (Tr. at 23, 25; I.J. at 2).
 
The respondent does not dispute that he did not submit any applications for relief when asked to by the Immigration Judge, and on appeal, he does not allege or establish that he is eligible for any relief. Therefore, we cannot conclude that, even assuming his attorney ineffective, there was prejudice. Although the respondent claims that his prior counsel should have established his eligibility for a U visa based on being a victim of a crime (Respondent’s Br. at 1-2, 8), the record does not contain sufficient evidence that the respondent would qualify for such a visa or that he has even applied for it. At any rate, we do not have jurisdiction to grant such relief. See Matter of Sanchez Sosa, 25 I&N Dec. 807 (BIA 2012) (discussing how an alien establishes prima facie eligibility for a U visa and whether a remand or continuance is warranted to await adjudication of such relief).
 
Likewise, although the respondent asserts that he is eligible for “prosecutorial discretion” (Respondent’s Br. at 1, 8), the Board and the Immigration Judges do not have authority to influence or require the Department of Homeland Security to exercise its discretion in deciding which cases to prosecute or not. See Reno v. American Arab Anti-Discrimination Committee, 525 U.S. 471, 488-91 (1999); Lopez-Telles v. INS, 564 F.2d 1302, 1304 (9th Cir. 1977). Although cancellation of removal was raised as a possible form of relief for which the respondent might be eligible (Tr. at 15), it was ultimately not pursued, and the respondent does not appear statutorily eligible for such relief (see Tr. at 24). See section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). Finally, it is not clear whether the respondent is statutorily eligible for voluntary departure, which the Immigration Judge denied as a matter of discretion (Tr. at 23; I.J. at 2), but the respondent does not raise this issue on appeal and, therefore, we decline to address it further.
 
*3 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 2835224 (BIA)
End of Document

© 2020 Thomson Reuters. No claim to original U.S. Government Works.






2012 WL 2835227 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OLIVIA BUKSH CORTEZ A.K.A. OLIVIA BUKAH CORTEZ
File: AXXX XX7 192 - Baltimore, Maryland
June 14, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kai W. Degraaf, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Carrie E. Johnston
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Termination
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from an Immigration Judge’s January 18, 2011, oral decision which found her subject to removal as charged. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a response on appeal in support of the Immigration Judge’s decision. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including the credibility determination, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i) (2012). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues involving questions of law, judgement and discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent, a 55-year-old native and citizen of Philippines, entered the United States in 2003 on a tourist’s visa. She eventually was hired as a live-in nanny by a United States citizen who led her to believe that she would assist her in becoming lawful permanent resident with the assistance of two prior attorneys. However, the petition for alien worker eventually was denied, and a Notice to Appear was issued in March 2009, which alleged that the respondent was subject to removal because she had overstayed her tourist visa. The respondent, through the same attorney who represents her on appeal, conceded the factual allegations and removability on August 31, 2010 (Tr. at 8).
 
On December 7, 2010, the respondent, through counsel, submitted a motion to terminate proceedings setting forth the preceding facts. He alleged that the respondent had been deprived of due process essentially because she had been misled by her employer and the prior attorneys and that the Notice to Appear had been “improvidently issued,” citing 8 C.F.R. § 239.2(a)(6). On December 8, 2010, the Immigration Judge denied the motion via a preprinted order in which he had handwritten that the respondent “has already admitted the allegations and conceded removability as charged.”
 
At a hearing on January 18, 2011, the respondent, through counsel, requested a final order of removal in order that he could pursue the possibility of deferred action, and he conceded that the respondent was not eligible for any form of relief. Therefore, the Immigration Judge entered an order of removal on January 18, 2011.
 
The respondent filed a Notice of Appeal which indicated that she was appealing the Immigration Judge’s removal order of January 18, 2011. However, counsel has submitted a brief on appeal which reiterates the arguments contained in the motion to terminate and alleges again that the Notice to Appear was improvidently issued. The respondent essentially argues that she should not have been placed in removal proceedings because she received ineffective assistance of counsel, without any legal support for this position. In addition, she maintains that she should not be required to comply with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) because the ineffective assistance in her case is plainly apparent.
 
*2 The respondent has misinterpreted the regulations and case law which permit the DHS or an Immigration Judge to cancel a Notice to Appear as improvidently issued. The regulations and case law do not permit an alien to request that a Notice to Appear be cancelled on this basis. See generally Matter of W-C-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 118 (BIA 2007); Matter of G-N-C-, 22 I&N Dec. 281 (BIA 1998); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 239.2(a)(6), (c).
 
In the circumstances, we will affirm the Immigration Judge’s decisions of December 8, 2010, which denied the motion to terminate, and January 18, 2011, which ordered the respondent removed. The respondent was represented by competent counsel who conceded the factual allegations and removability, she has not established that she is eligible for any form of relief, or that she is not subject to removal as charged.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 2835227 (BIA)

2012 WL 2835234 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WILKINS YEREMIS DE LOS SANTOS
File: AXXX XX6 570 - York, PA
June 12, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Salvador Delgado, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jon D. Staples
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)] - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
 
APPLICATION: Termination; remand
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s February 28, 2012, decision ordering his removal. During the pendency of his appeal, the respondent has also filed a motion to reopen, which we construe as a motion to remand. See generally Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1999). We review findings of fact by the Immigration Judge for clear error, while all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii). The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed and the motion denied.
 
The respondent concedes that he was convicted of criminal sale of marijuana in violation of New York Penal Law § 221.40 (2004) and of unlawful possession of marijuana in violation of New York Penal Law § 221.05 (2004). However, the respondent challenges the validity of these convictions, and argues that the Immigration Judge should not have relied on such convictions in sustaining the above charges of removability. The respondent also challenges the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that he has been convicted of an aggravated felony.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent is removable pursuant to section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i), as an alien who has any time after admission been convicted of a controlled substance violation, other than a single offense involving possession for one’s own use of 30 grams or less of marijuana. Although the respondent argues on appeal that he was not guilty as charged of the marijuana sale offense, it is well settled that neither an Immigration Judge nor this Board may look behind the fact of conviction and re-litigate the ultimate question of an alien’s guilt or innocence. See Matter of Roberts, 20 I&N Dec. 294 (BIA 1991). Similarly, although the respondent may assert that he received ineffective assistance of counsel during criminal proceedings, the respondent does not indicate that he has successfully pursued a collateral challenge to this offense in state court, and the conviction therefore supports a finding of removability in these proceedings. See Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992). Finally, although the respondent states that his convictions may have been expunged from his criminal record pursuant to a rehabilitative statute, an alien remains convicted of an offense for immigration purposes pursuant to the definition of “conviction” set forth in section 101(a)(48)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(48)(A), notwithstanding a subsequent vacatur under a state rehabilitative statute. See Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003). For these reasons, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent has been convicted of two controlled substance violations and is removable under section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Act.1
 
*2 We do not find merit in the respondent’s Motion to Reopen Deportation Proceedings.2 The respondent’s motion was initially filed with the Immigration Court during the pendency of this appeal. It was properly rejected by the Immigration Court because an Immigration Judge ceases to have jurisdiction over a motion to reopen at such time as jurisdiction vests with this Board. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b). A “motion to reopen” is not properly before the Board as the respondent’s motion was filed during the pendency of the present appeal, and thus at a time when there was no administratively final order of removal in his case. See 8 C.F.R. § 1241.1. To the extent that we therefore construe the respondent’s motion as a motion to remand, we conclude that the respondent has not demonstrated that remand is warranted. See Matter of Coelho, supra. While it appears that the respondent argues in his motion that he was inappropriately ordered removed in absentia, we note that the respondent was present and participated at his February 28, 2012, hearing. Further, although the respondent appears to raise an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, the respondent has not submitted evidence demonstrating compliance with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (enumerating evidentiary requirements for a party to prevail upon an ineffective assistance of counsel claim). For these reasons, we conclude that the respondent has not shown that remand is warranted in this case. The respondent’s request for a stay or removal is moot, and the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
Sharon Hoffman
FOR THE BOARD

We note that the respondent, who was admitted in 2002, cannot establish eligibility for cancellation of removal pursuant to section 240A(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a), because his 2004 convictions preclude him from accruing the required continuous residence. See section 240A(d)(1) of the Act.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JERMANINE RONNIE SALMON A.K.A. JERMAINE R SALMON A.K.A. TIKI SALMON A.K.A. TIKI R SALMON
File: AXXX XX2 933 - Newark, NJ
June 11, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Continuance; voluntary departure
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Jamaica, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge dated March 8, 2011, denying his request for a continuance and finding him removable as charged.1 During the pendency of this appeal, the respondent submitted new evidence that we construe as a motion to remand. The respondent’s request for oral argument is denied. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7). The respondent’s request for a waiver of the filing fees associated with the appeal is granted. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a)(1), 1003.8. The appeal will be dismissed and the motion will be denied.2
 
We review the findings of fact made by the Immigration Judge, including any determination of credibility, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge should have continued the proceedings since his post-conviction relief (“PCR”) was pending. He further argues that he entered defective guilty pleas due to ineffective assistance from his criminal counsel below, in light of Padilla v. Kentucky, ___ U.S. ___, 130 S.Ct 1473 (2010).3 The respondent contends that his constitutional rights were violated.
 
We find that the Immigration Judge appropriately went forward with the proceedings without granting the respondent an additional continuance (I.J. at 2-4; Tr. at 4-6 dated Mar. 8, 2011). The decision to grant or deny a continuance is within the discretion of the Immigration Judge, and good cause must be shown for a continuance. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29; Khan v. Att’y Gen. of the U.S., 448 F.3d 226, 233 (3d Cir. 2006); Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433, 434 (BIA 1987); Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354, 355-57 (BIA 1983). In this case, the Immigration Judge had continued the proceedings from December 14, 2010, until March 8, 2011, based on the respondent’s testimony that his PCR case for the 2008 conviction had been granted (I.J. at 2-3; see, e.g., Tr. at 31-33 dated Jan. 3, 2011). However, at the March 2011 hearing, the respondent proffered evidence indicating that criminal counsel had been appointed and his PCR case was pending (I.J. at 2-3; Tr. at 1-3 dated Mar. 8, 2011). The record also reflects that the respondent intended to pursue PCR for his 2007 conviction (Tr. at 5 dated Mar. 8, 2011). Thus, as the respondent’s ability to prevail on his PCR case was speculative, the Immigration Judge appropriately denied his request for a continuance. To date, the respondent has not proffered evidence to suggest that his PCR case for the 2008 conviction will be successful.
 
*2 Further, the fact that the respondent may be pursuing PCR in the form of a collateral attack for his 2008 conviction in state criminal court does not affect its finality for federal immigration purposes. See Paredes v. Att’y Gen. of the U.S., 528 F.3d 196, 198-99 (3d Cir. 2008) (stating the pendency of post-conviction motions or other forms of collateral attacks does not vitiate finality, unless and until the convictions are overturned as a result of the collateral motion); see also Matter of Rodriguez-Carrillo, 22 I&N Dec. 1031, 1034 (BIA 1999). The respondent did not present evidence with this appeal that any attack on his 2008 conviction resulted in any vacatur. Likewise, there is no evidence that the respondent’s 2007 conviction has been vacated. In Padilla v. Kentucky, supra, the United States Supreme Court ruled that an attorney representing an alien in connection with a guilty plea to a criminal offense has a constitutional duty to advise the alien about the risk of deportation arising from the conviction. However, the respondent’s appellate assertions that his convictions are invalid, in light of Padilla v. Kentucky, supra, does not change the finality of his convictions for immigration purposes, unless and until they are overturned by the criminal court.4 See Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992); Matter of Ponce De Leon-Ruiz, 21 I&N Dec. 154, 157 (A.G. 1997; BIA 1996). In other words, we are bound by the conviction records and we have no authority to vacate the convictions before us as the judgements do not appear to be a void on their face.
 
The record also does not support the respondent’s contentions that his constitutional rights were violated. To establish a violation of due process, the respondent must demonstrate that he was substantially prejudiced from the alleged procedural errors. See Leslie v. Att’y Gen. of the U.S., 611 F.3d 171, 175-76 (3d Cir. 2010); Khan v. Att’y Gen. of the U.S., supra, at 236. Here, the respondent was afforded a fair hearing to present his claims for relief. The Immigration Judge had continued the proceedings to determine the status of the respondent’s PCR case. The Immigration Judge had also determined that the respondent was statutorily ineligible for cancellation of removal, that he did not express any fears of persecution or torture if removed to Jamaica, and that he had misplaced his passport and was unable to pay for his travel (Tr. at 3-4, 6 dated Mar. 8, 2011). Based on this record, the respondent did not demonstrate that he was substantially prejudiced by the Immigration Judge, and therefore, we find no violation of due process.
 
*3 For the foregoing reasons, the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
To the extent the respondent seeks to apply for voluntary departure under section 240B(b), 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (the “Act”) and proffers new evidence, we construe it as a motion to remand and we deny the motion. Motions to remand are adjudicated under the same standards as are motions to reopen. See Matter of Ige, 20 I&N Dec. 880, 884 (BIA 1994). Such a motion may not be granted unless the evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). A motion to remand must conform to the same standards as a motion to reopen where the respondent seeks to present new evidence, but will only be granted if the new evidence “would likely change the result in the case.” See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 473 (BIA 1992).
 
Although the respondent now asserts that his fiancee holds his passport and that his family can pay for his travel, he has not established by clear and convincing evidence that he has the means to depart the United States and intends to do so. See section 240B(b)(D) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(c). The respondent bears the burden of proof to establish eligibility for voluntary departure. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.8(d); 1240.26(c). Aside from the respondent’s conclusory statements, the proffered statements from the respondents’s brother, cousin, father, grandmother, uncle, co-worker, and friends, do not demonstrate, by clear and convincing evidence, that the respondent has the financial means to depart voluntarily and intends to do so. See also Matter of Arguelles-Campos, 20 I&N Dec. 811 (BIA 1999). Rather, the proffered statements suggest that the respondent seeks another opportunity to remain in the United States. In his most recent submission filed with the Board on May 4, 2012, the respondent did not present any further evidence to establish that he is prima facie eligible for voluntary departure. Thus, we will deny the respondent’s motion.
 
Due to the nature of our disposition of this matter, we need not address the respondent’s other arguments on appeal and we find no basis to remand this case for further proceedings. See INS v. Bagamasbad, 429 U.S. 24, 25-26 (1976) (stating as a general rule, courts and agencies are not required to make findings on issues the decision of which is unnecessary to the results they reach); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462, 465 (BIA 2002). Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
*4 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD

We consider these issues to be waived. See Lie v. Ashcroft, 396 F.3d 530, 532 n.1 (3d Cir. 2005); Matter of J-Y-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 260, 261 n.1 (BIA 2007); Matter of Edwards, 20 I&N Dec. 191, 196-97 n.4 (BIA 1990).

IN RE: WAN SOO CHEE KUM NAM CHEE SUNG AE CHEE BONG SUK CHEE
Files: AXXX XX3 669 - San Francisco, CA
AXXX XX3 670
AXXX XX3 671
AXXX XX3 672
June 5, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Michael T. Purcell, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Grace H. Cheung
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondents are natives and citizens of South Korea. This proceeding involved Leland Sustaire, a former federal government employee who obtained lawful permanent resident status for a number of aliens, including the respondents here, even though there was no underlying basis for such status (Exhs. 4-5). The respondents conceded removability and did not seek any relief so the Immigration Judge ordered them removed.
 
The respondents appealed and on March 27, 2006, the Board dismissed the respondents’ appeals. The respondents filed a timely petition for review. On August 10, 2011, the Ninth Circuit dismissed the petition for review. Chee et al. v. Holder, No. 06-71952 (9th Cir. Aug. 8, 2011). The Ninth Circuit held that equitable estoppel against the government was not available where the respondents did not lose any rights to which they were entitled. The Ninth Circuit held that the respondents’ remaining contentions were not persuasive.
 
After receiving the 9th Circuit’s decision, the respondents hired new counsel and on November 17, 2011, they filed a motion to reopen our March 2006 decision. The motion is untimely. Section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c).
 
In the motion, the lead male respondent (AXXX XXX XXX) and his wife (AXXX XXX XXX) request that the Board exercise its sua sponte authority to reopen proceedings. Their children (AXXX XXX XXX and AXXX XXX XXX), as well as AXXX XXX XXX on their behalf, contend that their former attorney provided ineffective assistance of counsel because they were unaware that they qualified for post-conclusion voluntary departure, and had they known that they qualified, they would have insisted that their former counsel request it, rather than withdraw the request. In addition, AXXX XXX XXX seeks sua sponte reopening so that he may pursue adjustment of status based on an pending I-130 visa petition filed by his United States citizen wife. The Department of Homeland Security has filed an opposition to the motion.
 
The motion will be denied as untimely. The respondents AXXX XXX XXX and AXXX XXX XXX do not contend that ineffective assistance by former counsel is apparent on the face of the record. As such, the respondents had to substantially comply with the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The respondents attached a complaint and cover letter, but they did not submit sufficient evidence demonstrating that a complaint was filed with the appropriate bar office. The respondents did not attach, or supplement the record, with the certified mail receipt, the return receipt, or an acknowledgment letter from the state bar.
 
*2 Furthermore, even assuming substantial compliance with Lozada, the respondents have not demonstrated that former counsel provided ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondents claim that they were not aware of their eligibility for voluntary departure, and that they would have requested such relief had they been aware. Such an assertion comes 5 years after the Board dismissed the appeal. In light of the record and former counsel’s response, see DHS Opposition, Exh. A, their assertions are inherently not believable. Former counsel states that the respondents AXXX XXX XXX and AXXX XXX XXX did not want to pursue voluntary departure because they wanted to stay and apply for relief (DHS Opposition, Exh. A).
 
According to the transcript of proceedings, former counsel declined voluntary departure on the respondents’ behalf and did not seek any other applications for relief such as adjustment of status based on their labor because they were not eligible for adjustment at the time, but they hoped to seek relief in the future (Tr. at 147-48). Respondents, by their own admissions, were present during this discussion. Further, the respondents did not have a basis for asylum, withholding or protection under the Convention Against Torture (Tr. at 149). Their former counsel’s strategic decisions did not constitute ineffective assistance, but rather a strategic decision based on his understanding of the respondents’ wishes during the proceedings. Torres-Chavez v. Holder, 567 F.3d 1096, 1101 (9th Cir. 2009) (care must be taken to not distort a lawyer’s decisions with the benefit of hindsight).
 
Given the respondents’ (AXXX XXX XXX and AXXX XXX XXX) desire to remain in the United States and their hope to adjust their status, it was a reasonable litigation tactic to withdraw the pending voluntary departure requests as a failure to comply with the stringent time constraints of voluntary departure would result in a bar on adjustment, among other restrictions, for 10 years. Section 240B(d) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d).
 
The respondents AXXX XXX XXX, AXXX XXX XXX, and AXXX XXX XXX seek reopening as a matter of discretion. We decline to exercise our sua sponte discretion to reopen because the respondents have not demonstrated exceptional circumstances. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Becoming eligible for adjustment subsequent to a final administrative order of removal is not unusual and is not by itself an exceptional circumstance.
 
Accordingly, the following orders shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The request for a stay is denied as moot.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: ABDEL RAZEK SAAD ZAGHLOUL AHMED

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ismael Gonzalez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Claire W. Matecko
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Egypt, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated February 28, 2011, denying his motion to reconsider. We will dismiss the respondent’s appeal.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
The respondent was ordered removed in absentia by an Immigration Judge on October 6, 1998. See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(6)(A). On October 27, 2010, the respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Court. On December 2, 2010, the Immigration Court mailed the Immigration Judge’s decision, dated December 1, 2010, denying the motion to reopen to the parties. On January 4, 2011, 33 days after the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the motion to reopen was mailed, the respondent filed a “Motion to Reconsider Reopening Removal Proceedings” wherein he “move[d] for reconsideration of his motion to reopen removal proceedings” (Respondent’s Motion to Reconsider at 2).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reconsider. The Immigration Judge properly recognized that the respondent’s motion to reconsider was untimely as it was filed more than 30 days after the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen (I.J. at 2). See section 240(c)(6)(B) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). The respondent does not dispute that his motion to reconsider was untimely. See Claudio v. Holder, 601 F.3d 316, 319 (5th Cir. 2010) (holding that once an alien elects in his notice of appeal to file a brief, that brief becomes the operative document through which any issues that the alien wishes to have considered must be raised). While the respondent does claim that his removal would result in hardship to his family and he has other equities in the United States, the power to reconsider cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). As such, sua sponte reconsideration was not warranted.
 
On appeal, the respondent attempts to challenge the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny his motion to reopen. For example, he argues that the untimeliness of his motion to reopen was justified, that his motion to reopen complied with the evidentiary requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and that he is eligible to adjust status under section 245(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(i) (Respondent’s Br. at 1-5).1 However, the respondent did not appeal the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny his motion to reopen. As such, any arguments concerning the merits of the motion to reopen are not properly before us. Instead, the only decision currently before us for the purposes of appellate review is the Immigration Judge’s decision, dated February 28, 2011, which properly denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider as untimely. Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent does not allege that the attorney who filed the motion to reconsider provided ineffective assistance of counsel by not timely filing the motion or allege that he has fulfilled the evidentiary requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, in order to properly raise such a claim.

2012 WL 2870244 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSUE ELIAS MUNGUIA-SALAZAR
File: AXXX XX4 461 - New Orleans, LA
May 31, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)] - In the United States in violation of law
 
 
APPLICATION: Voluntary departure
 
The respondent has filed an appeal of the Immigration Judge’s January 24, 2012, decision granting him voluntary departure. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not filed a response to the respondent’s appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The hearing transcript reveals that the respondent, through counsel, requested only the relief of pre-conclusion voluntary departure for a period of 90 days (Tr. at 7-10), a grant of which requires that the alien waive “appeal of all issues.” Section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b)(1)(i)(D); see also Matter of Ocampo, 22 I&N Dec. 1301 (BIA 2000) (holding that 120 days’ voluntary departure may not be granted prior to the completion of removal proceedings without an express waiver of the right to appeal). The transcript further reflects that, after explaining the order, the Immigration Judge asked the respondent’s counsel whether the respondent waived appeal, to which the respondent’s counsel responded “He does, Your Honor.” (Tr. at 10, lines 9-12).
 
On February 23, 2012, the respondent nevertheless filed a Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26), contending that he was not advised during the proceedings that he was required to waive his appeal rights in order to accept pre-conclusion voluntary departure; and he did not receive an adequate explanation regarding the ramifications of waiving appeal. However, as set out above, the respondent, with counsel present, was explicitly advised that he was required to waive his appeal rights in order to accept pre-conclusion voluntary departure. See Kohwarienv. Holder, 635F. 3d 174 (5th Cir. 2011) (finding that an alien accepting pre-conclusion voluntary departure knowingly and intelligently waived his appellate rights). To the extent the respondent’s appeal can be construed as alleging ineffective assistance of counsel, he has complied with none of the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) for making such a claim. See also Mai v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 162, 165 (5th Cir. 2006).
 
Consequently, the respondent, through counsel, knowingly and intelligently waived appeal. The Immigration Judge’s decision became administratively final upon the respondent’s waiver of the right to appeal, and that decision remains the final administrative order in these proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a)(1) (final sentence), 1003.39 (finality of order); Matter of Rodriguez-Diaz, 22 I&N Dec. 1320 (BIA 2000); Matter of Shih, 20 I&N Dec. 697 (BIA 1993). Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further Board action.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 2870244 (BIA)
End of Document

2012 WL 2253017 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE LUIS HUIPIO DELGADO
File: AXXX XX0 124 - Houston, TX
May 18, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Steven P. Mock, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Wm. Tracy Hamby
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals the Immigration Judge’s decision, dated January 11, 2010, denying his motion to reopen. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
A motion to reopen must state the new facts that will be proven at the reopened hearing and be supported by affidavits or other evidentiary materials demonstrating prima facie eligibility for the relief sought. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). An alien who seeks to remand or to reopen proceedings to pursue relief bears a heavy burden of proving that if the proceedings were reopened, with all the attendant delays, the new evidence would likely change the result in the case. See Matter of Coelho, supra.
 
For the first time on appeal, the respondent alleges that an individual who is not a member of the State Bar of Texas purported to represent him at a scheduling hearing, and for that reason, his counsel of record was uninformed concerning the next pertinent date in the proceedings, causing him to miss a critical deadline. First, the respondent himself was present at the hearing where the Immigration Judge provided the date at issue (Tr. at 9-10). The heavy burden for prevailing on a motion to reopen cannot be shifted away from the respondent due to his failure to relate this information to his counsel of record. Next, the respondent made no effort to comply with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), with respect to any ineffective assistance of counsel claim he intended to pursue. Finally, respondent’s counsel of record was on notice, due to the lapse of time, that some intervening court action might have occurred, but failed to investigate. Therefore, the respondent’s motion to reopen warrants denial, as he did not establish that new information affected the outcome of his case. Matter of Coelho, supra
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
*2 Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 2253017 (BIA)
End of Document

2012 WL 8305101 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FRANCISCO ISMAEL DIAZ-BISONO
File: AXXX XX7 740 - Atlanta, GA
May 16, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sarah Owings, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Joy Lampley
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent is a citizen of the Dominican Republic. On November 22, 2011, an Immigration Judge granted him the opportunity to depart the United States voluntarily by January 23, 2012, under safeguards. The respondent filed a motion to reopen on January 17, 2012. The Immigration Judge denied it on February 27, 2012, and this appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008). These proceedings fall within the REAL ID Act.
 
The respondent argues on appeal that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his motion to reopen. He disputes the Immigration Judge’s finding that he did not substantially comply with the procedural requirements for establishing ineffective assistance of counsel as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See also Dakane v. U.S. Atty.Gen., 399 F.3d 1269 (11th Cir. 2005).
 
Even assuming that the respondent satisfied the procedural requirements for his claim, he must also establish that his former counsel’s performance was deficient to the point that it impinged on the fundamental fairness of the hearing. See Ali v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 643 F.3d 1324, 1329 (11th Cir. 2011). It is required that he establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s alleged ineffectiveness. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The degree of negligence that must be established for ineffectiveness is that counsel’s representation was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 (BIA 1998).
 
In the instant case, the respondent has not established that he was prima facie eligible for a grant of any form of relief from removal at his hearing other than voluntary departure. In fact, the respondent does not even address the issue with particularity in either his brief or the Notice of Appeal. Instead, the respondent’s contentions are limited to whether he satisfied the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. He has therefore not demonstrated that he was prejudiced by his former counsel’s representation.
 
*2 We acknowledge that the respondent asserted in his motion to reopen that his former counsel’s inaction deprived him of the opportunity to be considered at the hearing for a “stand alone” waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. 1182(h) (See motion at 5). However, he is not eligible to be granted the waiver.
 
In Matter of Abosi and Matter of Sanchez, the Board found that an alien could apply for a “stand alone” 212(h) waiver, where the alien was a returning legal permanent resident seeking to overcome a ground of inadmissibility. However, an alien, like the respondent, who is within the United States and charged with deportability must submit an application for a 212(h) waiver concurrently with an application for a visa, admission, or adjustment of status. See section 212(h)(2) of the Act (a waiver of inadmissibility is permitted where the ““““Attorney General ... has consented to the alien’s applying or reapplying for a visa, for admission to the United States, or adjustment of status); 8 C.F.R. § 1245.1(f) (“an application under this part shall be the sole method of requesting the exercise of discretion under sections 212(g), (h), (i), and (k) as they relate to the inadmissibility of an alien in the United States”).
 
The respondent is not an alien seeking admission into the United States and would not be applying for the waiver of inadmissibility in conjunction was an application for adjustment of status. In fact, the respondent acknowledges in his motion that he could not adjust his status to a lawful permanent resident of the United States in the foreseeable future because of his visa priority date. We do not find that the respondent established that he was denied a fair hearing warranting reopening of these proceedings. The Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 8305101 (BIA)
End of Document

2012 WL 1951064 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TOMASZ JAN DIDENKO JOLANTA BRONISLAWA DIDENKO
Files: AXXX XX2 180 - Memphis, TN
AXXX XX2 179
May 3, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Agnieszka Haupt, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Scot A. Bearup
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The respondents, both natives and citizens of Poland, appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated October 20, 2010, which denied their request for a continuance but granted voluntary departure under section 240B of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c. The request for oral argument is denied. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but we review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii). Upon de novo review, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s denial of a continuance. Although the respondents argue on appeal that there was good cause to grant a continuance based on alleged ineffective assistance of counsel in visa petition proceedings, they have not filed, either before the Immigration Judge or the Board, any documents showing that they are pursuing the ineffective assistance claim or that such a claim is factually supported. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1998), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In any event, inasmuch as the alleged ineffective assistance relates to an employment-based visa petition, such matter is outside the scope of the Immigration Judge’s and the Board’s jurisdiction. See Matter of Hernandez-Puente, 20 I&N Dec. 335, 339 (BIA 1991); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(b), 1240.1(a)(1).
 
We also agree with the Immigration Judge that any possible favorable change in immigration law is speculative. Moreover, to date, the respondents have not filed an application for relief or otherwise identified any relief from removal for which they may be eligible. Thus, the respondents have not shown that there was good cause for a continuance or that the Immigration Judge’s denial of a continuance resulted in actual prejudice. Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433, 434 (BIA 1987); Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354, 356 (BIA 1983); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29. See Al-Ghorbani v. Holder, 585 F.3d 980, 992 (6th Cir. 2009). Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondents are permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondents fail to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondents shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondents fail to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondents shall be subject to a civil penalty as provided by the regulations and the statute and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
WARNING: If the respondents file a motion to reopen or reconsider prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period set forth above, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated; the period allowed for voluntary departure is not stayed, tolled, or extended. If the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated upon the filing of a motion, the penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(e)(1).
 
WARNING: If, prior to departing the United States, the respondents file any judicial challenge to this administratively final order, such as a petition for review pursuant to section 242 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated, and the alternate order of removal shall immediately take effect. However, if the respondents file a petition for review and then departs the United States within 30 days of such filing, the respondent will not be deemed to have departed under an order of removal if the alien provides to the DHS such evidence of his or her departure that the Immigration and Customs Enforcement Field Office Director of the DHS may require and provides evidence DHS deems sufficient that he or she has remained outside of the United States. The penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply to an alien who files a petition for review, notwithstanding any period of time that he or she remains in the United States while the petition for review is pending. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(i).

IN RE: SERGIO ANTONIO FUENTES-CHICAS

MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se1
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Margaret R. Kugel
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 Before us for decision is the respondent’s motion to reopen seeking an opportunity to apply for special rule cancellation of removal under section 203 of the Nicaraguan Adjustment and Central American Relief Act, Pub. L. No. 105-100, 111 Stat. 2193, 2196, amended by Pub. L. No. 105-139, 111 Stat. 2644 (1997) (NACARA). The motion, which is opposed by the DHS, will be denied.
 
The instant motion (filed December 1, 2011), is untimely by more than 9 years. Section 240(c)(7)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2); August 9, 2002, Board dec. (final administrative decision).2 There also has been no showing that at the late filing of this motion is excused. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(3)(i)-(iv).
 
In addition, we decline to reopen the respondent’s proceedings sua sponte, inasmuch as he has not demonstrated the existence of exceptional circumstances warranting such action. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999). Further, notwithstanding the currently-advanced assertions to the contrary, the respondent, a citizen of El Salvador, has not shown prima facie eligibility for special rule cancellation of removal.3 He entered the United States in March 1985, and therefore is an “ABC class member.” See American Baptist Churches, et al. v. Thornburgh, et al., 760 F. Supp. 796 (N.D. Cal. 1991) (ABC); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.60. However, there has been no additional evidentiary showing that he is a “registered ABC class member,” as defined in 8 C.F.R. § 1240.60 (requiring a person, such as the respondent, to have registered for ABC benefits with the former INS, or to have applied for Temporary Protected Status (TPS),4 on or before October 31, 1991). As such, he does not qualify as a section 203 NACARA principal applicant under 8 C.F.R. § 1240.61(a)(1). Further, the respondent has not established that he applied for asylum or before April 1, 1990, such that he qualifies as a principal applicant for such relief under 8 C.F.R. § 1240.61(a)(2).
 
*2 Insofar as it is also asserted that the respondent received ineffective assistance by his former attorney, this allegation has been advanced only by Mr. Chukwurah, not by the respondent himself. See Respondent’s Response to Government’s Opposition to Motion to Reopen at 2. Moreover, in moving to reopen the respondent has not submitted any evidence reflecting his compliance with the requirements for presenting an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, as delineated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied. The request for a stay of removal is also denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The request for a stay of removal pending adjudication of the respondent’s motion to reopen is denied as moot.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LEONARDO GUTIERREZ A.K.A. LEONARDO GUITERREZ FLORES
File: AXXX XX9 223 - San Antonio, TX
April 25, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
*1 This case was last before us on March 21, 2011, at which time we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s September 28, 2010, denial of his request for a continuance. The respondent has now filed an untimely motion to reopen proceedings on December 27, 2011. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent argues that he suffered ineffective assistance of counsel in his previous proceedings because his counsel before the Immigration Judge failed to timely apply for withholding of removal, failed to obtain a bond for the respondent, and failed to appeal the Immigration Judge’s decision. He further argues that his attorney before the Board was not licensed as an attorney in the state of Texas, and also argues that she failed to raise an ineffective assistance of counsel claim during the appeal process. In support of his argument, the respondent has submitted an affidavit, a copy of the client agreement between himself and Ronald Higgins, Esquire, and evidence that he has filed complaints against both of his prior attorneys. The respondent has also filed evidence that he has pursued post-collateral relief regarding his conviction.
 
Notwithstanding this evidence, we do not find that the respondent has demonstrated prejudice as a result of his attorneys’ actions. See generally Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The respondent has not submitted evidence that he contracted with his first attorney to represent him on appeal, and inasmuch as this Board accepted the respondent’s untimely brief and considered his appeal on the merits, we do not find that he was prejudiced by any change in representation. Moreover, the respondent has not submitted an agreement with his prior appellate counsel.
 
Further, the respondent has failed to file an application for withholding of removal, as required by the regulations governing motions to reopen, nor has he set forth any kind of persecution claim. See § 1003.2(c)(1). The respondent has not shown that he was prejudiced by his first attorney’s failure to pursue withholding of removal on his behalf, or by his appellate attorney’s failure to raise his ineffective assistance of counsel claim. The respondent has also not demonstrated how he was affected by his appellate attorney’s licensure in New York rather than Texas, and the respondent has submitted insufficient evidence regarding the nature of his representation during his bond proceedings. Lastly, we note that while the respondent has proffered documentation that he has filed a petition for writ of habeas corpus with the United States District Court, Middle District of Florida, Orlando Division, he has not submitted any indication that the Court changed the disposition of his conviction in alignment with Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003); see also Matter of Chavez, 24 I&N Dec. 272 (BIA 2007).
 
*2 Accordingly, we find no basis upon which to grant the respondent’s untimely motion to reopen proceedings based on his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See generally Matter of Lozada, supra; Matter of Assaad, supra. The motion will, thus, be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Molly Kendall-Clark

2012 WL 1705618 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MAURICIO CLODOMIRO VELEZ-DELGADO
File: AXXX XX8 502 - Newark, NJ
April 23, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Teodora Arias, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Patricia M. Medeiros
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 On March 15, 2012, the respondent submitted an untimely motion to reopen proceedings in which his appeal was dismissed by the Board on March 28, 2008. He also seeks a stay of removal. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes reopening. The motion is denied.
 
The respondent asks that he be considered for “prosecutorial discretion” pursuant to the “Morton Memorandum” of November 17, 2011 (Motion at 3). He further contends that his attorney, Mr. Pollack, did not inform him of the Board’s adverse decision. However, the respondent has not complied with the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, as enumerated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), nor has he made any showing of prejudice, and reopening on this basis is not warranted. Rranci v. Att’y Gen., 540 F.3d 165, 172 (3d Cir. 2008) (outlining those requirements); Lu v. Ashcroft, 259 F.3d 127, 129 (3d Cir. 2001) (holding that “the Lozada requirements are a reasonable exercise of the Board’s discretion”).
 
The respondent further avers he is a “perfect candidate for prosecutorial discretion” as outlined in the “Morton Memorandum” (Motion at 3). However, this Board is not authorized to take action on such a request. Rather, a request for the favorable exercise of prosecutorial discretion can be directed to the DHS. While the respondent also cites humanitarian factors in asking that proceedings be reopened, these factors do not constitute an exception to the statutory time limitation.
 
As the respondent has not demonstrated that his motion is exempt from the time limits on motions to reopen, neither reopening nor a stay of removal is warranted. Accordingly, the motion is denied.
 
Molly Kendall-Clark
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 1705618 (BIA)

APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Vietnam, has appealed from the decision of the Immigration Judge dated June 18, 2010. 2012 WL 1705614 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BEATRICE AKUMU ONONO
File: AXXX XX7 955 - Baltimore, MD
April 20, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Steven P. Barsamian, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent is a native and citizen of Kenya. In a decision issued May 9, 2000, an Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed in absentia. The respondent filed a motion to reopen. She conceded that she had notice of the hearing, but averred that she was unable to travel due to illness. The Immigration Judge denied the motion because the respondent had not demonstrated exceptional circumstances. The respondent filed a Notice of Appeal, and on April 20, 2001, the Board dismissed the appeal.
 
Over 11 years later, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. She alleges that the time within which to reopen should be equitably tolled due to ineffective assistance of counsel. She also seeks reopening under the Board’s sua sponte discretionary authority. The motion to reopen is denied as untimely. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c).
 
The respondent has not satisfied the requirements of equitable tolling. She has not demonstrated substantial compliance with the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Barry v. Gonzales, 445 F.3d 741, 745-46 (4th Cir. 2006). Nor has the respondent demonstrated that she exercised due diligence in seeking reopening. See Chao v. Virginia Department of Transportation, 291 F.3d 276, 283 (4th Cir. 2002)(equitable tolling is not available when a party fails to exercise due diligence on his own behalf).
 
The limited evidence proffered with the motion, considered with the other evidence of record, does not demonstrate an exceptional situation that would warrant the exercise of our discretion to reopen proceedings under our sua sponte authority. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, the motion is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 1705614 (BIA)

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JEFFRY DEJESUS GONZALEZ PENA A.K.A. JEFFREY GONZALEZ
File: AXXX XX9 205 - Boston, MA
April 19, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jeffrey B. Rubin, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 This case was previously before the Board on February 27, 2012, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s November 3, 2011, decision finding him removable as charged and ordering him removed from the United States. On March 15, 2012, the respondent filed a timely motion to reconsider and reopen with the Board alleging that his former counsel did not file an appellate brief on his behalf.1 The respondent also claims that his current counsel will file a motion to withdraw his guilty plea with the Lawrence District Court. The Department of Homeland Security has not filed a brief in opposition to the motion. The motion will be denied.2
 
To the extent the motion is a motion to reconsider, it does not specify errors of fact or law in our former decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1) (stating that a motion to reconsider shall specify “errors of fact or law in the prior Board decision and shall be supported by pertinent authority.”); Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). To the extent the motion is a motion to reopen, it is not supported with the requisite evidence. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1) (stating that a motion to reopen “shall be supported by affidavits or other evidentiary material.”). The respondent’s counsel’s statements in the brief are not evidence. See INS v. Phinpathya, 464 U.S. 183, 188-89 n.6 (1984); Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980); Matter of S-M-, 22 I&N Dec. 49, 51 (BIA 1998). Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reconsider and reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes

2

On March 19, 2012, the Board denied the respondent’s request to stay removal.

2012 WL 1705670 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: COMFORT AFUA AFARBEA KOLI A.K.A COMFORT AFARIBEA WOODS
File: AXXX XX3 114 - Cleveland, OH
April 11, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Floyd J. Fernandez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; rescission
 
*1 On June 22, 2010, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen and rescind an order of removal entered in absentia on July 26, 2007. See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A). The respondent claimed that she did not receive proper notice of her removal hearing. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). On April 29, 2011, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision, noting that the Notice to Appear (“NTA”) was sent to the respondent at the address she provided. On November 2, 2011, the respondent filed the instant untimely motion to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3)(i). The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent, who sought and continues to seek adjustment of status as the spouse of a United States citizen, raises ineffective assistance of counsel claims in the instant motion. Although she alleges that her former attorneys performed deficiently in both visa petition and removal proceedings, we emphasize that visa petition proceedings are separate and distinct from these removal proceedings. The dispositive issue now before the Board is whether ineffective assistance of counsel prevented the respondent from attending the scheduled removal hearing on July 26, 2007.
 
An alien may move to reopen and rescind an in absentia order of removal at any time if she did not receive proper notice. See section 240(c)(5)(C)(ii) of the Act. An alien may also move to reopen and rescind an in absentia order of removal within 180 days after date of the in absentia order if she establishes that the failure to appear was due to exceptional circumstances. See section 240(c)(5)(C)(i) of the Act. The respondent now seeks to toll the filing deadline in light of her claim that she did not receive the NTA due to ineffective assistance of counsel.1 See Motion to Reopen at 3.
 
The respondent claims that her former attorney, Rateb Khasawneh, received a copy of the NTA in May 2006, and that he failed to notify her of the scheduled removal hearing. Id., at 6-7. She also alleges that Emmanuel Olawale, Esquire, the attorney who filed the motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge and the appeal before the Board, failed to raise the issue of Rateb Khasawneh’s alleged failure regarding the NTA. See Motion Exhibit A-2, at 2-3.
 
Initially, we find that the respondent has not complied with the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel before this Board as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). See Pepaj v. Mukasey, 509 F.3d 725, 727 (6th Cir. 2007) (“An alien who fails to comply with Lozada’s requirements forfeits her ineffective-assistance-of-counsel claim”). We find no evidence of Rateb Khasawneh’s representation of the respondent in the record, and we find no evidence of the respondent’s compliance with Lozada’s requirements with regard to her claim of ineffective of counsel against him. With regard to her claim against Attorney Olawale, the respondent has not offered evidence that she filed a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authorities against Attorney Olawale in compliance with Lozada’s requirements. We further note that in his response to the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim, Attorney Olawale explains that he learned from the respondent after the dismissal of her appeal that Rateb Khasawneh had received a copy of the NTA. Attorney Olawale disputes the respondent’s claims of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*2 In sum, the respondent has not set forth viable claims of ineffective assistance of counsel. Further, even if the respondent had complied with Lozada’s requirements, and even if she had shown (which she has not) that she suffered prejudice as a result of ineffective assistance of counsel, equitable tolling is not appropriate because the respondent has not shown that she has acted with due diligence in bringing her ineffective assistance of counsel claims before the Board. See Barry v. Mukasey, 524 F.3d 721, 724 (6th Cir. 2008). We note that in making her initial motion to reopen to rescind, no claim of ineffective assistance of counsel was made.
 
Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied. The respondent’s request for a stay of removal is also denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Molly Kendall-Clark
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent concedes that she was no longer living at her address of record at the time the NTA was mailed to her. See Respondent’s Motion at 4-5. She specifically claims that she did not receive the NTA because she had moved. Id. Although she states that she notified the U.S. Postal Service that she had moved, the respondent has neither claimed nor provided evidence that she notified the Department of Homeland Security of her change of address.
