2012 WL 1705669 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALFONSO GASPAR-VELARDE A.K.A. ALFONSO GASPAR-VELARDO A.K.A. JOSE GASPAR-BELARDO
File: AXXX XX6 001 - Miami, FL
April 11, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Andrew Brown
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico who entered the United States without inspection by an immigration officer, appeals the Immigration Judge’s decision, dated January 10, 2012, ordering his removal from this country. We will dismiss the respondent’s appeal.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
It is undisputed that the respondent is subject to removal from the United States as a result of his manner of entry into this country (I.J. at 2-3; Exh. 1).1 See section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i). As the respondent is subject to removal, it is his burden to establish eligibility for relief from removal. See section 240(c)(4)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(4)(A); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(d). On appeal, the respondent does not allege that he is eligible for any form of relief from removal that can be granted by an Immigration Judge or this Board.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to enter an order of removal in this matter in lieu of further continuing proceedings. An Immigration Judge may grant a continuance where good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. An Immigration Judge’s denial of a continuance will not be overturned unless the respondent establishes that the denial deprived him of his due process right to a full and fair hearing. See Matter of Luviano, 21 I&N Dec. 235, 237 (BIA 1996) (citing Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433 (BIA 1987)).
 
In this case, the respondent was personally served with a Notice to Appear on June 17, 2011 (Exh. 1). The respondent was granted continuances on July 21, 2011 (Tr. at 2), August 22, 2011 (Tr. at 4, 9-10), November 3, 2011 (Tr. at 12), November 29, 2011 (Tr. at 13), and January 3, 2012 (Tr. at 15). Additionally, the Immigration Judge granted the respondent’s written motion for a continuance in October 2011.
 
Despite having been afforded a significant amount of time to prepare for his removal proceedings, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge should have further continued removal proceedings as he desires to apply for a U visa with United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (“USCIS”) under the provisions of section 101(a)(15)(U) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(15)(U), he had recently hired an unnamed “new attorney,” and that his prior counsel was ineffective. The respondent argues that his prior counsel provided ineffective assistance by not appearing at scheduled hearings and not properly presenting and pursuing his claim for a U visa.2
 
*2 USCIS has “sole jurisdiction” over all applications for U visa status and an alien who is subject to a final order of removal is not precluded from filing such an application. 8 C.F.R. § 214.14(c)(1). Additionally, 8 C.F.R. § 214.14(c)(1)(ii) explicitly provides that, while the pendency of an application for a U visa has no effect on the Government’s authority to execute a removal order, an applicant for a U visa is permitted to file a request for a stay of removal with the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). If USCIS approves the respondent’s application, he may file a motion to reopen and terminate removal proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 214.14(c)(5)(i). Even if the respondent departs the United States, he may also pursue his application from abroad. 8 C.F.R. §214.14(c)(5)(i)(B). Considering the significant number of procedural safeguards which are in place in order to allow an alien a reasonable opportunity to pursue a U visa subsequent to an order of removal being entered against him, as well as the significant amount of time that was already provided to the respondent to pursue a U visa, we do not consider the speculative possibility that the respondent will be granted a U visa to be good cause to further continue these proceedings.3 See generally Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103, 110 (BIA 2009) (“As a practical matter, Immigration Judges and the Board have limited and finite adjudicative and administrative resources, and those resources are best allocated to matters over which we do have jurisdiction.”).
 
While the respondent claims that he had recently hired new counsel, he does not state the name of the representative that he allegedly hired, he has not presented any evidence to support such claims, and has not explained why these removal proceedings should have been continued in order for this unnamed attorney to enter an appearance. As discussed above, the respondent does not dispute that he is subject to removal from the United States and does not allege eligibility for any form of relief from removal that can be granted by an Immigration Judge or this Board. As such, the respondent’s claims concerning a “new attorney” do not establish that a further continuance was warranted. Such claims are also insufficient to establish that the unnamed attorney was ineffective.
 
To the extent that the respondent argues that his former counsel was ineffective, we observe that the respondent has not presented (1) an affidavit setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with his former counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, (2) evidence that his former counsel has been informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) any indication as to whether a complaint against his former counsel has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
*3 Additionally, the respondent’s claims of ineffective assistance of counsel do not amount to a basis to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision as the respondent has not demonstrated that he was prejudiced by his counsel’s conduct. See Ali v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 643 F.3d 1324, 1329 (11th Cir. 2011). Given that the respondent does not dispute that he is subject to removal from the United States and does not allege eligibility for any form of relief that can be granted by an Immigration Judge or this Board, the respondent has not demonstrated that a reasonable probability that but for the counsel’s alleged errors, the outcome of the proceedings would have been different. Even if an ineffective assistance claim could be premised on an counsel’s conduct regarding issues which are not directly related to removal proceedings, i.e., the pursuit of a U visa, the respondent has not demonstrated that an authorized official is willing to “certify” the respondent for the issuance of a U visa. See 8 C.F.R. § 214.14(c)(2)(i). The former counsel’s assertions indicate that he made efforts to procure a certification from the state attorney’s office in Orange County, Florida and that the office was unresponsive (Tr. at 13, 16). The mere fact that the state attorney’s office has declined to issue a certification to the respondent does not established that the respondent’s counsel was ineffective. The respondent has not established that, even his former counsel’s assistance was free from the alleged defects, there is a reasonable probability that the state attorney’s office would have issued the requisite certification.
 
On appeal, the respondent has presented documents relating to his pursuit of the requisite certification needed for a U visa. The Board does not consider evidence first provided on appeal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv). Additionally, we decline to remand these proceedings to the Immigration Judge to consider the evidence in the first instance as such evidence has not been shown to affect the outcome of this case. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 471 (BIA 1992). As discussed above, USCIS has sole jurisdiction to grant a U visa. Moreover, the evidence offered by the respondent does not demonstrate that an authorized official is willing to certify the respondent for U visa status. Accordingly, the evidence offer does not establish that the Immigration Judge’s decision to order the respondent removed to Mexico should be disturbed. The following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Elise L. Manuel
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

As the respondent is subject to removal as a result of entering the United States without inspection by an immigration officer, we need not further address whether he is also subject to removal as a result of his criminal history.

2

The record reflects that the respondent’s former counsel did not timely appear at the removal hearing scheduled on August 22, 2011 (Tr. at 4). However, he appeared later that day (Tr. at 6). The respondent’s former counsel did not appear at the hearing on January 3, 2012 (Tr. at 15).

3

To date, the respondent has not presented any evidence that he has actually applied for a U visa.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SUNDAY UKWU
File: AXXX XX2 493 - Dallas, TX
April 6, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Margot Merrill-Johnson
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 This case was last before us on March 25, 2002, at which time we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s February 17, 1999, decision ordering him removed from the United States to Nigeria. The respondent has now filed an untimely motion to reopen proceedings on January 13, 2012. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent argues that we should reopen proceedings based on alleged ineffective assistance of counsel that he received from the attorney who represented him before the Immigration Court and this Board. Specifically, the respondent argues, inter alia, that prior counsel (1) did not timely notify the respondent of the Board’s 2002 decision, (2) did not timely file an appeal to the Circuit Court, and (3) did not challenge the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent is removable as an aggravated felon.1
 
The respondent has provided no evidence in support of these allegations and claims, except for his own declaration, which neither identifies his prior counsel nor sets forth any agreement between himself and prior counsel. Thus, notwithstanding the respondent’s declaration, he has not complied with the guidelines set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) in making claims against his former counsel. Matter of Lozada, supra, requires that the alien must lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between the alien and the representative; the alien must give the representative the opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffectiveness, and the alien must file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made.
 
While the respondent argues that his unidentified prior attorney failed to promptly notify him of the issuance of our decision or appeal its outcome, he has filed insufficient evidence for us to ascertain how this delay indicates that he suffered any prejudice on account of such allegations. See id. Lastly, the respondent has failed to show due diligence in making such claims against prior counsel, inasmuch as he has filed this motion over nearly 13 years after the Immigration Judge’s decision, and nearly a decade after our decision in the case. See id; Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). As such, we do not find that equitable tolling is warranted, nor do we find that the respondent warrants reopening under our sua sponte authority pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). See generally Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, we will deny the respondent’s motion as untimely.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Molly Kendal-Clark
FOR THE BOARD

1

The respondent’s appellate brief contained a legal argument disputing the respondent’s removability as an aggravated felon, and we addressed this issue in our 2002 decision (Bd. Dec. at 1-2). In our decision, we noted that the aggravated felony charges against the respondent had not been sustained by the Immigration Judge, and the DHS had not appealed from the Immigration Judge’s rulings in this regard.

2012 WL 1495505 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EVA LONGORIA-GARCIA A.K.A. EVA GARCIA VARGAS
File: AXXX XX9 451 - Harlingen, TX
April 3, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Abigail D. Molina, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Nicole M. Gonzalez
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, was ordered removed in absentia on March 17, 2011. On April 4, 2011, the respondent filed a motion to reopen proceedings, which the Immigration Judge denied on April 21, 2011. The respondent filed a timely appeal of that decision. The appeal will be sustained, proceedings will be reopened and the record will be remanded.
 
The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen finding that she had received proper notice for her March 17, 2011, hearing. We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), are satisfied.
 
Upon review, we find that in view of the above and in light of the totality of circumstances presented in this case, including evidence reflecting that the respondent’s former counsel conceded that he provided ineffective assistance by failing to appear at the March 17, 2011, hearing, we will allow the respondent another opportunity to appear for a hearing. Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665 (BIA 2008).
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained, proceedings are reopened and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
Elise L. Manuel
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 1495505 (BIA)

2012 WL 1495576 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TANIESHA CAMILLA COPE
File: AXXX XX4 676 - Miami, FL
March 28, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
James J. Orlow, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Paulette R. Taylor
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On October 24, 2011, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision denying her motion to reopen. The respondent filed her second motion to reopen on November 23, 2011, which the Department of Homeland Security opposes. She submitted supplemental documentation on February 13, 2012. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion is both time and number-barred as it is her second such motion, and was filed more than 90 days after the Immigration Judge’s June 10, 2010, final administrative decision. Sections 240(c)(7)(A), (C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(7)(A), (C)(i); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c), 1003.23(c). The respondent seeks reopening as she avers that former counsel, Ms. Clarke, provided her with ineffective assistance. Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269 (11th Cir. 2005); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). She also asks that proceedings be reopened sua sponte. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a).
 
While the respondent has largely complied with the procedural requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, she did not demonstrate that she was prejudiced by Ms. Clarke’s actions. Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., supra at 1274 (holding that, “in addition to substantial, if not exact, compliance with ... Lozada, a petitioner ... must also show prejudice” in order to establish the ineffective assistance of counsel in the context of removal proceedings). “Prejudice exists when the performance of counsel is so inadequate that there is a reasonable probability that but for the attorney’s error, the outcome of the proceedings would have been different.” Id.
 
One of the reasons why the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s September 28, 2010, motion to reopen was that she failed to submit evidence regarding the bona fides of her marriage. The respondent has not shown that such evidence was available and could have been presented at that time. With her current motion the respondent submitted a sworn affidavit reporting that she met her husband in November of 2009, and that they were married on August 29, 2010. She provides no details regarding their courtship or relationship. However, the record reflects that the respondent sought a continuance on January 27, 2010, because she was then pregnant, and had moved to New York “where the father of her baby resides.” She stated that he was “supporting her emotionally and financially.” The motion for the continuance makes no mention of the respondent’s current husband, nor has he been identified as the father of the respondent’s baby. There is also no indication that the respondent’s husband ever resided in New York. Rather, it appears that he has always resided in Pennsylvania. Submissions from the respondent and her husband since the motion for a continuance do not mention either the baby, or the identity of the baby’s father.
 
*2 The respondent’s husband offered a statement dated December 9, 2010, more than a year after they reportedly met and more than three months after their marriage, indicating that they could not provide some documents in support of their marriage “due to incidents not cleared up yet” (Motion, Tab D). The evidence that was submitted included a November 2, 2010, life insurance policy naming the respondent as one of the beneficiaries; a December 9, 2010, form from a utility company indicating that the respondent had applied for service at a Pennsylvania address; a June 15, 2010, receipt for wedding rings from a Pennsylvania store; and photocopies of three wedding cards and a picture of the married couple (Motion, Tabs E-G).
 
As noted, the Immigration Judge denied reopening in part because of the respondent’s failure to submit evidence that her marriage was bona fide. With the 2010 motion, Ms. Clarke submitted a statement from the respondent, evidence that her husband had filed a visa petition on her behalf, and a copy of the marriage certificate. The only other evidence that apparently was available and could have been submitted in conjunction with the motion filed by Ms. Clarke would have been a letter from the respondent’s husband, and the photocopies of the cards and picture of the couple. There is no evidence that her husband’s letter would have been substantially different from the one submitted by the respondent. We find that, even if such evidence had been submitted, it would have been insufficient to establish the bona fides of the marital relationship. Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 2002). As such, the respondent has not demonstrated that Ms. Clarke’s performance was so inadequate that there was a reasonable possibility that, but for her failure to submit the available evidence, the outcome of the proceedings would have been different. Ali v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 643 F.3d 1324, 1329 (11th Cir. 2011). Therefore, reopening on this basis is not warranted.
 
Similarly, we decline to reopen proceedings sua sponte. The fact that the visa petition was approved on January 26, 2012, more than a year after the Immigration Judge denied her motion to reopen and more than three months after the Board upheld that decision, does not constitute an exceptional circumstance supporting reopening. Rather, the approval of a visa petition months after a respondent has been ordered removed is a not uncommon, rather than exceptional, circumstance. As such, we decline to exercise our discretionary authority to sua sponte reopen proceedings. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (the Board’s sua sponte authority is limited to exceptional situations and is not meant to circumvent the time limits where enforcing them might cause hardship); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a).
 
*3 As we find that reopening is not warranted, the following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 1495576 (BIA)
End of Document

© 2020 Thomson Reuters. No claim to original U.S. Government Works.






2012 WL 1495577 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NORIKO KANAI A.K.A. NORIKO KANAI-POARCH
File: AXXX XX3 177 - Omaha, Nebraska
March 28, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Victor T. Nevada, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Clete P. Samson
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a 39-year-old married female native and citizen of Japan, has appealed from an Immigration Judge’s written decision dated November 11, 2010, which denied her motion to reopen her in absentia removal order. The respondent also filed an “amended” motion, and a motion to reconsider, which we will construe as constituting motions to remand. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a response on appeal. The motions will be denied. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including the credibility determination, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i) (2011). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues involving questions of law, judgement and discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
An order of removal issued following proceedings conducted in absentia pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A), may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances, because she did not receive proper notice of the hearing, or because she was in federal or state custody and failed to appear through no fault of her own. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995); Matter of Gonzalez-Lopez, 20 I&N Dec. 644 (BIA 1993).
 
Following a hearing conducted in absentia on September 28, 2004, when the respondent failed to appear, the Immigration Judge found the respondent subject to removal as charged, determined that she had abandoned all potential applications for relief, and ordered her removed from the United States. See generally section 240 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a.
 
In her motion to reopen filed on October 12, 2010, which was 6 years after she had been ordered removed, she alleged, through counsel Jacob Chang, that she had not received notice of her September 28, 2004, hearing.1 She also alleged that she had been instructed by her previous immigration attorney “of record” that it was not necessary for her to appear at the hearing.
 
*2 In a written decision issued on November 11, 2010, the Immigration Judge denied the motion because the notice of her hearing had been mailed to the respondent’s last known address as indicated in the Record of Deportable/Inadmissible Alien (Form I-213) in the record (Exh. 3). In addition, he noted that the respondent was not represented by counsel at the time the in absentia order was entered and therefore no attorney of record existed. Insofar as she had alleged that a prior attorney had committed ineffective assistance, the Immigration Judge found that the motion did not comply with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) because it did not reflect that she had filed a bar complaint. Finally, he noted that the motion did not contain an affidavit from the respondent.
 
On November 8, 2010, the respondent, through new attorneys, Maile M. Hirota and Victor T. Nevada, filed an “amended” motion to reopen with the Omaha Immigration Court.2 However, it was not received by the Chicago Immigration Court, where the Immigration Judge was located, until November 16, 2010, after he had issued his decision on the first motion. On November 29, 2010, she filed a motion to reconsider, which was not addressed by the Immigration Judge before the Notice of Appeal was filed with the Board on December 7, 2010. Therefore, we will construe both motions as constituting motions to remand before the Board.
 
In the November 8, 2010, motion, the respondent claimed that her original motion contained “material inaccuracies and omissions” because she had, in fact, received notice of the September 28, 2004, hearing. She therefore withdrew her claim of lack of notice. Instead, she maintained that she should be eligible for equitable tolling based on the alleged ineffective assistance of an attorney (Thomas Lee) she and her husband had retained to assist them in several matters, including her immigration case, and that his ineffective assistance constituted exceptional circumstances. The respondent’s November 29, 2010, motion requested that the Immigration Judge vacate his November 11, 2010, decision because her request to substitute counsel had been incorrectly rejected based on the absence of a pending case.
 
On appeal, the respondent alleges that it was error for the Immigration Judge to deny her motion to reopen without having reviewed her “amended” motion to reopen, and that she has established exceptional circumstances and ineffective assistance of counsel to merit reopening of her in absentia removal order.
 
We will affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s October 12, 2010 motion. The Immigration Judge properly found that the respondent had received notice of the September 28, 2004, hearing and the respondent conceded that she received notice in her November 8, 2010, motion. In addition, the motion did not contain an affidavit as required, and it also did not establish any exceptional circumstances to excuse her failure to appear. Matter of W-F-, 21 I&N Dec. 503, 509 (BIA 1996); Section 240(e)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(l).
 
*3 Furthermore, the respondent has not set forth any valid reason to excuse her failure to comply with Matter of Lozada, supra, when she filed her October 12, 2010, motion to reopen she should have also made her ineffective assistance claim. See Matter of Medrano, 20 I&N Dec. 216 (BIA 1990) (arguments for consideration should be presented at one time, and not in “piecemeal fashion”).
 
We will deny the respondent’s November 8, 2010, motion because the respondent has not satisfied all of the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, even at this time, because she has not provided a copy of the bar complaint she allegedly filed against Mr. Lee, and only has provided a copy of the bar complaint her husband filed against him for his representation in non-immigration matters. She also has not provided a copy of Mr. Lee’s response, if any, to her bar complaint, nor has she alleged that she has filed a bar complaint against Mr. Chang, who filed the first motion to reopen which contained factual inaccuracies.
 
To the extent that the respondent claimed that she was not aware of the allegations made by Mr. Chang in her October 12, 2010, motion, aliens “are generally bound by the conduct of their attorneys, including admissions made by them, absent egregious circumstances.” Magallanes-Damian v. INS, 783 F.2d 931, 934 (9th Cir. 1986). Furthermore, her contention that the Immigration Judge erred by issuing his decision without reviewing her November 8, 2010, motion is without merit because the Immigration Judge was not aware that she submitted a subsequent motion.
 
We also will deny the respondent’s November 29, 2010, motion regarding the rejection of her motion to substitute counsel by the Omaha Immigration Court administrative staff. The rejection represents at most a harmless clerical error, and the respondent has not demonstrated any prejudice because her new attorneys later filed two motions which were accepted. Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s November 8, and November 29, 2010, motions to remand are denied.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD

On November 3, 2010, the respondent filed a motion for substitution of counsel to replace Mr. Chang with Maile M. Hirota and Victor T. Nevada. IN RE: PEDRO MORALES GONZALEZ A.K.A. WILBER REYNA MORALES A.K.A. PEDRO AMILCAR GONZALEZ
File: AXXX XX8 170 - El Paso, TX
March 23, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
William M. Hunt
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I)] - Crime involving moral turpitude
 
Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Remand
 
The appeal is dismissed. The Immigration Judge’s order, dated July 8, 2010, reflects that the respondent had waived appeal, and the transcript of proceedings also indicates that the respondent, through his attorney, waived appeal (Tr. at 12). On July 22, 2010, the respondent nevertheless filed a Notice of Appeal.
 
The Immigration Judge’s decision became administratively final upon the respondent’s waiver of the right to appeal, and the Board lacks jurisdiction over this case. See Matter of Rodriguez-Diaz, 22 I&N Dec. 1320, 1322 (BIA 2000); Matter of Shih, 20 I&N Dec. 697 (BIA 1993); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(2)(G), 1003.39. The respondent makes no claim that the appeal waiver was invalid.
 
To the extent that the respondent claims ineffective assistance of counsel, he has not complied with any of the requirements for making such a claim, as described in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See also Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487, 498 (5th Cir. 2000) (upholding the Lozada requirements). These requirements include filing an affidavit describing the scope of the representation agreement, notifying the prior attorney of the allegations, and lodging a complaint against the attorney with the proper disciplinary authorities. Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639.1
 
Therefore, we find that this case is not properly before us, and the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied, the appeal is dismissed, and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without any further Board action.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD

Also, as a longtime resident of the United States who wrote his Notice of Appeal and extension request in English, there is no evidence that he was hampered in understanding the proceedings.

IN RE: MEI QIN JIANG

MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mark J. Falco, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Sarah B. Campbell
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on June 18, 2001, at which time we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s denial of her motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia removal order that was entered on October 4, 2000. On May 20, 2011, the respondent filed a new motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order of removal based on a deficiency with the Notice to Appear (“NTA”) and ineffective assistance of counsel. The motion is properly filed with the Board, since we entered the last order in these proceedings. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
As an initial matter, the respondent urges that the NTA should be deemed invalid because it did not specify the date and time of her initial hearing (Motion at 4). We note that the NTA indicates both the date and time are “to be determined” (Exh. 1). Pursuant to the applicable regulation, the date and time of an initial removal hearing shall be provided in the NTA “where practicable,” and if the date and time are not set when the NTA is issued, the Immigration Court shall be responsible for scheduling the hearing and providing notice regarding the date, place, and time of the initial hearing. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.18(b). Accordingly, here, in compliance with these regulations and the Act the Immigration Court sent the respondent’s initial notice of hearing to the address of the detention center where she was currently residing.1 See section 239(a)(2)(A), (c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a)(2)(A), (c) (permitting service by mail of the time and place of any hearing before the Immigration Court and finding attempted service at the alien’s last address provided in accordance with section 239(a)(1)(F) of the Act to be sufficient).
 
In the alternative, the respondent urges that the numerical limitations on motions to reopen and rescind an in absentia order should be equitably tolled because she is the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i) and 240(c)(7)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i) & (c)(7)(A) (setting forth time and number limits on motions to reopen and rescind in absentia orders of removal); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(3) & 1003.23(b)(4)(ii) (same); see also Zhao v. INS, 452 F.3d 154, 159 (2d Cir. 2006) (finding that an ineffective assistance of counsel claim may warrant equitable tolling of both the time and number limits).
 
*2 First, the respondent has not complied with any of the Lozada2 procedural requirements. See Jiang v. Mukasey, 522 F.3d 266, 270 (2d Cir. 2008) (stating that a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel must comply with the Lozada requirements). Second, the respondent has not demonstrated that she acted with due diligence in pursuing her claim prior to filing the instant motion on May 20, 2011, almost 10 years after the Board’s prior decision. See Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 130-32 (2d Cir. 2008) (finding that, in order to warrant equitable tolling, an alien is required to demonstrate “due diligence” in pursuing his claims during “both the period of time before the ineffective assistance of counsel was or should have been discovered and the period from that point until the motion to reopen is filed”). Third, the respondent has not demonstrated that she was prejudiced by her prior counsels actions or inactions. See Jiang v. Mukasey, supra, at 270 (stating that to succeed on a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance an applicant must show, inter alia, “that the applicant was prejudiced by his counsel’s performance.”). Here, the respondent seeks to apply for political asylum. However, she has not articulated a basis for her claim nor has she submitted an asylum application or any evidence to establish her prima facie eligibility for relief. Thus, the motion to reopen is untimely, and the respondent has not shown due diligence or prejudice to warrant equitable tolling based on ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
Finally, although not explicitly requested, the respondent has not shown that an “exceptional situation” exists that would warrant the Board’s exercise of its discretion to reopen these proceedings sua sponte. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (stating that “as a general matter, we invoke our sua sponte authority sparingly, treating it not as a general remedy for any hardships created by enforcement of time and number limits in the motions regulations, but as an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations”). Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent was initially placed in removal proceedings with the Immigration Court in Miami, Florida. She was then released from former INS custody and filed a motion to change venue to the Immigration Court in New York, New York (see I.J. Decision dated October 4, 2000; Exh. 2).

2

Matter of Lozada, 19 I & N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), requires a successful ineffective assistance of counsel claim be accompanied by: “(1) an affidavit setting forth in detail the agreement with former counsel concerning what action would be taken and what counsel did or did not represent in this regard; (2) proof that the alien notified former counsel of the allegations of ineffective assistance and allowed counsel an opportunity to respond; and (3) if a violation of ethical or legal responsibilities is claimed, a statement as to whether the alien filed a complaint with any disciplinary authority regarding counsel’s conduct and, if a complaint was not filed, an explanation for not doing so.”).

IN RE: RITO RAMIREZ A.K.A. RITO RAMIREZ-SAUCEDO

March 13, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Sarosh Nawaz Wahla
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Voluntary departure
 
*1 In a decision dated November 29, 2011, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s request for voluntary departure and ordered him removed to Mexico. The respondent filed a timely appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, under a clearly erroneous standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s denial of voluntary departure. The Immigration Judge weighed the positive and negative equities. See Matter of Lemhammad, 20 I&N Dec. 316 (BIA 1991). We have considered the respondent’s arguments and find no clear error in the Immigration Judge’s findings of fact. Upon de novo review of whether those facts support the conclusion that the respondent failed to meet his burden of proof to establish that he merits relief as a matter of discretion, we reach the same conclusion as the Immigration Judge. The respondent essentially argues on appeal that the two controlled substances crimes discussed by the Immigration Judge, as well as a misdemeanor driving under the influence offense that the Immigration Judge did not discuss, are insufficient for a negative finding. The respondent also discusses the length of time he has spent in the United States and the economic difficulties he would face in Mexico. While we acknowledge that the respondent has presented some positive equities, we conclude that, under the circumstances of this case, the respondent does not merit a grant of voluntary departure.
 
The respondent states that the Immigration Judge should have continued the case until his attorney made an appearance, but he does not state that he requested a continuance, and does not describe what, if any, efforts he made to have an attorney represent him. The transcript reflects that the respondent was able to communicate with the Immigration Judge and there was no apparent reason to continue the case based on the respondent’s inability to proceed pro se. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29 (continuance may be granted for good cause shown). Thus, contrary to the respondent’s appellate claims regarding due process and the right to counsel, we find no constitutional violation. To the extent that the respondent’s appellate argument may be construed as a complaint about the performance of his counsel, he has not filed a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, and on the record before us we see no justification for sustaining the appeal on this basis. See, e.g. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
*2 Based on the foregoing, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

2012 WL 911853 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SHAWN SHELDON ALI
File: AXXX XX5 070 - Newark, NJ
March 9, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se1
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Patricia M. Medeiros
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 In an oral decision dated November 14, 2011, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s request for a continuance; found him removable; determined that he did not demonstrate eligibility for any relief from removal; and ordered him removed from the United States to Trinidad and Tobago.2 The respondent appealed from that decision. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent was found removable as charged, as in the United States in violation of law pursuant to section 237(a)(1)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B). Upon our de novo review, we find correct the Immigration Judge’s conclusions concerning the respondent’s removability and ineligibility for removal relief.
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge should have granted him an additional continuance of his removal hearings to await the adjudication by the criminal court of pending charges for criminal possession of stolen property and assault. At his removal hearings, the respondent indicated that, if the criminal charges were dismissed and he were released from custody, he would marry his United States citizen fiancee, who would file an immediate relative visa petition on his behalf. In addition, his lawful permanent resident mother had filed a second preference visa petition for him on November 4, 2011. If a visa petition for him were approved, he would seek adjustment of status under section 245 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255. He stated that he was not seeking any other relief from removal. See Tr. at 4. He was represented by immigration counsel throughout his hearings. With his appeal, he has provided documentation from the criminal court reflecting that the assault charge was dismissed without prejudice on December 20, 2011, and that the criminal possession of stolen property charge is to be dismissed and sealed on March 1, 2012.
 
The decision to grant or deny a continuance is within the discretion of the Immigration Judge, and good cause must be shown for a continuance. See Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433 (BIA 1997); Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354 (BIA 1983); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29. In this case, the Immigration Judge afforded the respondent two previous continuances, from September 27, 2011, until October 31, 2011; and from October 31, 2011, until November 14, 2011. As of the November 2011 final hearing, no visa petition had been approved for the respondent. The preference petition filed for him by his mother in November 2011 remained pending. He and his citizen fiancee had not married and, consequently, she had not even filed a spousal petition for him. In the absence of an approved visa petition, the respondent did not demonstrate eligibility for adjustment of status, consistent with the Immigration Judge’s determinations.3 Given the lack of an approved visa petition, the respondent is ineligible for adjustment of status, even if both of his criminal charges have been dismissed by the criminal court. Cf. Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009) (holding that, if the respondent presents a prima facie approvable visa petition and an adjustment application, a rebuttable presumption arises that the continuance should be granted and that, in considering a continuance request, four factors to be evaluated are: 1) the position of the DHS regarding the continuance request; 2) whether the visa petition is prima facie approvable; 3) whether the respondent is statutorily eligible for the requested relief; and 4) whether discretion should be exercised in the respondent’s favor). We agree with the Immigration Judge’s determination not to allow a third continuance.
 
*2 Next, the respondent contends that he received ineffective assistance of counsel from the attorney who represented him at his removal hearings. However, the respondent has failed to offer any evidence that substantially complies with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and he further has failed to demonstrate that he suffered the requisite prejudice from counsel’s alleged action or inaction such that a remand for consideration of his claim would be warranted, based on his cursory and unsupported appellate allegations. See Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (directing the Board and Immigration Judges to apply “the Lozada framework” to claims of ineffective assistance of counsel “pending the issuance of a final rule”), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009). The respondent has not demonstrated prima facie eligibility for adjustment or removal relief, either before the Immigration Judge or on appeal. To the extent that the respondent contends that his removal proceedings were unfair, we find that he has not demonstrated any error by the Immigration Judge in handling his hearings, under the circumstances of this case. We also find that he has not demonstrated any resultant prejudice such as would amount to a due process violation. See Romanishyn v. Attorney General of U.S., 455 F.3d 175 (3d Cir. 2006).
 
Finally, the respondent has requested a remand. See Br. at 5. We do not find that a remand is warranted for any reason in this case, and we deny the remand request.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.4
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: WALTER ORLANDO CHIVICHON-HERNANDEZ A.K.A. WALTER CHIVICHON
File: AXXX XX1 448 - Imperial, CA
February 27, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Julie R. Sparks, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
W.P. Hollerich
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a citizen of Guatemala, was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on February 4, 2009, after failing to appear at a hearing. He was thereafter removed from this country, returned, and filed a motion to reopen on November 14, 2011. The Immigration Judge denied it on November 21, 2011, and this appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The respondent does not argue in his Notice of Appeal that his failure to appear at his hearing was caused by either “exceptional circumstances” or lack of notice. He states therein that he was eligible to apply for a U visa at the time of his hearing, and he was denied the opportunity.
 
The regulation at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) provides that “except as provided in paragraph (b)(4)(ii) of this section, a party may file only one motion to reopen proceedings (whether before the Board or the Immigration Judge) and that motion must be filed not later than 90 days after the date on which the final administrative decision was rendered in the proceeding sought to be reopened, or before September 30, 1996, whichever is later.” The respondent’s motion to reopen was filed nearly 2 years after the Immigration Judge ordered his removal from the United States. It is therefore untimely.
 
In addition, in Matter of Armendarez-Mendez, 24 I&N Dec. 646 (BIA 2008), the Board held that it lacked authority pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(d) to reopen removal, deportation, or exclusion proceedings-whether on motion of an alien or sua sponte-if the alien has departed the United States after those administrative proceedings have been completed. The only exceptions involve timely motions to reopen a removal order or alleging lack of notice as the reason for a failure to appear. See Martinez Coyt v. Holder, 593 F.3d 902 (9th Cir. 2010); Matter of Bulnes, 25 I&N Dec. 57 (BIA 2009). Neither situation is applicable herein.
 
*2 We further would not reopen these proceedings sua sponte even if we had such authority. We do not find that the respondent established the existence of an exceptional situation to warrant doing so. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). We also note no evidence that the respondent was denied due process by the Immigration Judge presiding at his hearing or satisfied any of the procedural requirements for establishing that he is a victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 911847 (BIA)
End of Document

2012 WL 691458 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KANG IL KIM MOON JA KIM A.K.A. MOON JA KIM KIM HYA RANG KIM
Files: AXXX XX6 920 - Hartford, CT
AXXX XX6 921
AXXX XX5 566
February 23, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Bongseob Moon, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lincoln Jalelian
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The respondents, natives and citizens of South Korea, have appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 29, 2009. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) seeks summary affirmance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
Suspension of deportation under section 244(a) of the Act maybe available to certain aliens in deportation proceedings. However, the respondents here are in removal proceedings. Removal proceedings replaced deportation proceedings in immigration cases commencing on or after April 1, 1997. See Illegal Immigration Reform and Immigrant Responsibility Act of 1996, Division C of Pub. L. No. 104-208, 110 Stat. 3009-546 (enacted Sept. 30, 1996) (IIRIRA). In addition, §§ 304(a)(3) and 308(a)(7) of the IIRIRA, 110 Stat. at 3009-594, 3009-615, specifically repealed section 244(a) of the Act in all cases commencing on or after April 1, 1997, and provided a new form of relief - cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) - for aliens in removal proceedings. The Notice to Appear (Form I-862) was served on the respondents on March 5, 2009, and filed with the Immigration Court on March 13, 2009. Thus, the respondents cannot apply for suspension of deportation under section 244(a) of the Act because that provision no longer existed for aliens, such as the respondents, who are in removal proceedings. See Arenas-Yepes v. Gonzales, 421 F.3d 111, 116-17 (2d Cir. 2005) (holding that because petitioner’s proceedings commenced after the enactment of IIRIRA, alien was statutorily ineligible for suspension of deportation). Moreover, they are statutorily ineligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Act because they have not shown that they have a qualifying family member (Tr. at 12-14). See section 240A(b)(1)(D) of the Act. Nor did they seek this form of relief. See Pleadings, dated September 23, 2009. Subsequent dissatisfaction with a strategic decision of counsel is not grounds for reopening. Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309, 310 (BIA 1998).
 
The respondents on appeal maintain that the Immigration Judge failed to consider that they “were defrauded by an immigration broker.” Notice of Appeal Addendum. The record does not reveal that this issue was raised before the Immigration Judge. In addition, we note that they have not complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), to establish a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Jian Yun Zheng v. U.S. Dep’t of Justice, 409 F.3d 43, 46 (2d Cir. 2005); Matter of Asaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
*2 Finally, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondents failed to show any due process denial because they were not provided the list of free legal services with the Notice to Appear (I.J. at 1-3). The record reflects that the list was provided to the respondents at their first hearing on April 14, 2009 (Tr. at 3). Moreover, they have not established any prejudice, given that they were provided two continuances to obtain counsel (Tr. at 4, 7) and had representation at their final hearing of September 29, 2009 (Tr. at 9). See Monter v. Gonzales, 430 F.3d 546 (2d Cir. 2005)(to demonstrate “prejudice,” of kind required to entitle alien to remand based on immigration judge’s erroneous decision of motion for change of venue, alien must show that denial of venue change affected either the outcome or overall fairness of proceeding).
 
For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in his decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5).
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 691458 (BIA)
End of Document

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EUN JUNG NAM A.K.A. EUN JUNG NAM KIM
File: AXXX XX6 835 - Newark, NJ
February 10, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Harlan E. Schackner, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Patricia M. Medeiros
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was before the Board on December 18, 2009, when we issued our order denying the respondent’s motion for remand to seek adjustment of status, and dismissing her appeal from the Immigration Judge’s February 20, 2008, denial of her motion for a continuance. On April 19, 2011, the respondent filed the instant motion to reopen these removal proceedings to pursue adjustment of status. The motion is untimely. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i) (90-day time limit for motion to reopen); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (same). The respondent asserts that the filing deadline has been tolled in her case due to ineffective assistance of counsel. The Department of Homeland Security has filed a memorandum opposing the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The Board’s previous order denied the respondent’s motion to remand and dismissed her appeal for lack of jurisdiction. See Board Order, 12/18/2009 at 2. The respondent is an arriving alien. See Exh. 1A. The Board based its decision in her case on Matter of Silitonga, 25 I&N Dec. 89 (BIA 2009), holding that Immigration Judges have no jurisdiction to adjudicate an application of an arriving alien for adjustment of status, with an exception that we determined to be inapplicable based on the record here. The instant motion fails to address the matter of jurisdiction.
 
Assuming, however, that for purpose of this motion the Board should exercise jurisdiction, the respondent fails to make a persuasive argument that she is entitled to equitable tolling of the filing deadline on account of ineffective assistance of counsel. The Board held in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), that due process rights may be violated when ineffective assistance of counsel prevents an alien from reasonably presenting her case, but the alien must show that she was prejudiced by counsel’s performance by demonstrating eligibility for relief, and must meet procedural requirements, including a detailed statement of the agreement entered into with former counsel, notice to former counsel and the opportunity to respond, and a complaint filed with disciplinary authorities. The instant motion includes very brief complaints filed with New York disciplinary authorities against four attorneys on April 8, 2011 (days before filing of the motion). See Motion to Reopen (untabbed exhibits). These complaints lack detail as called for by the complaint form itself as well as Lozada, and do not include any supporting documentation. The Board concludes that the respondent has not demonstrated substantial compliance with the Lozada requirements. Further, the complaints and the instant motion were brought 16 months after the Board’s last order in the case, a delay that indicates lack of due diligence. See Mahmood v. Gonzales, 427 F.3d 248, 252-53 (3rd Cir. 2005) (whether alien exercised due diligence is ordinarily a fact question, but 13-month delay in moving to reopen was conclusive so as to deny equitable tolling, despite alien’s difficulty in “navigating the shoals of American law” (citation omitted)). We conclude that the filing deadline for a motion to reopen has not been tolled in this case.
 
*2 Finally, the Board concludes that the instant motion does not indicate any exceptional situation for the Board to grant her request to reopen these proceedings sua sponte. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (the Board’s sua sponte authority is an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations, and is not a general remedy for hardships created by enforcement of the time limit in the regulations).
 
Accordingly, the motion will be denied as untimely filed.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen and motion for a stay are denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ROBINSON CORDERO PEREZ
File: AXXX XX9 416 - Guaynabo, PR
January 31, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rosaura Gonzalez Rucci, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Magdalena Ramos Romey
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated June 3, 2010. The appeal will be sustained and the record will be returned to the Immigration Court for further proceedings.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under a clearly erroneous standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629, 637 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The record reflects that the Immigration Judge instructed the respondent to file his application for relief from removal in the form of adjustment of status by April 29, 2010. The respondent failed to comply with the filing deadline and he moved, through counsel, for a continuance and an extension of the filing deadline. Counsel indicated that she had been waiting for the respondent to supply a sponsorship affidavit in support of the application and simply “overlooked” the filing deadline. The Immigration Judge, in a decision date May 4, 2010, denied the motion indicating that insufficient reasons for failure to comply with the filing deadline were presented and declared the application for adjustment abandoned. The respondent filed a motion to reopen together with supporting documentation and a statement from counsel attributing the failure to file the application to her deficient performance. On May 17, 2010, the Immigration Judge denied reopening citing the respondent’s failure to submit an adjustment application with the motion, and incorporating the rationale of her May 4, 2010, decision. The Immigration Judge did not address counsel’s admission of ineffective assistance.
 
Upon our de novo review, we will reverse the decision of the Immigration Judge. We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not demonstrate sufficient reason for failing to file the respondent’s adjustment application. However, the respondent has also presented a plain instance of ineffective assistance of counsel that resulted in substantial prejudice, and we find that reopening is warranted on that basis. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). Inasmuch as counsel conceded responsibility for missing the filing deadline, we do not find that strict compliance with the procedural requirements for claims of ineffective assistance set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), is necessary. Compare Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309, 311 (BIA 1998) (declining to consider a claim of ineffective assistance in the absence of Lozada compliance when there was no showing of egregious conduct on the part of counsel). The record will be returned to the Immigration Court to provide the respondent with a further opportunity to seek relief from removal.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained and the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.
 
Sharon C. Hoffman
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 527500 (BIA)

2012 WL 527506 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MILAGRO GERVACIO-PIMENTEL
File: AXXX XX8 078 - Guaynabo, PR
January 30, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Luis A. Defillo-Rosas, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Ronald B. Seely
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on September 29, 2011, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal. On October 24, 2011, the respondent filed a timely motion to reconsider and reopen urging that she received ineffective assistance of counsel and that proceedings should be reopened for reconsideration of her application for adjustment of status. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has filed a brief in opposition to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
A motion to reconsider shall specify “errors of fact or law in the prior Board decision and shall be supported by pertinent authority.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b) (providing that a motion to reconsider shall specify “errors of fact or law in the prior Board decision and shall be supported by pertinent authority”); see also Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). The respondent’s motion does not establish any error in our previous decision concluding that she is statutorily ineligible for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(i). Moreover, the respondent does not argue that there was any error in the Board’s prior decision, but rather, requests that we reconsider our decision in light of the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The motion does not provide any basis to conclude error in the Board’s ultimate decision; therefore, the motion to reconsider will be denied.
 
The motion is also in the nature of a motion to reopen. Applicable regulations provide that a motion to reopen “shall state the new facts that will be proven at a hearing to be held if the motion is granted.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). Moreover, a movant must satisfy the “heavy burden” of establishing that if the proceedings were reopened, with all the attendant delays, the new evidence would likely change the result in the case. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). To prevail on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, an alien must show that competent counsel would have acted otherwise and that the alien was prejudiced. See Saakin v. INS, 252 F.3d 21, 25 (1st Cir. 2001); see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
The respondent urges that the attorney who represented her at her hearing below and on appeal before the Board was ineffective because he failed to request nunc pro tunc an I-212 waiver for permission to reapply for admission into the United States after removal (Motion at 2). First, we note that the respondent was found to be inadmissible under section 212(a)(9)(C)(i)(I) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(9)(C)(i)(I), for being in the United States for an aggregate period of more than 1 year and then re-entering the United States without being admitted.1 There is no waiver of inadmissibility available for section 212(a)(9)(C)(i)(I) of the Act. Matter of Briones, 24 I&N Dec. 355 (BIA 2007). To the extent that the respondent is arguing that a nunc pro tunc waiver of inadmissibility for illegal entry and status violations exists, we explained in Matter of Torres-Garcia, 23 I&N Dec. 866, 877 (BIA 2006), that the waiver available at 8 C.F.R. § 212.2 does not relate to the grounds of inadmissibility under section 212(a)(9)(C) of the Act. As the respondent remains ineligible for adjustment of status, she has not demonstrated prejudice from counsel’s actions or omissions such that she has established a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*2 Finally, while the respondent requests sua sponte reopening, her motion does not demonstrate an exceptional situation that would warrant the exercise of our discretionary authority. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (stating that “the power to reopen on our own motion is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship”). Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EUGENE EUSTACE MAKUPA
File: AXXX XX8 702 - Kansas City, MO
January 25, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Matthew L. Hoppock, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jennifer A. May
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Tanzania, has appealed from an Immigration Judge’s decision dated April 23, 2010, granting him voluntary departure and noting both the respondent and the Department of Homeland Security had waived their right to appeal.1 The appeal will be considered and dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including determinations of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether or not the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge should have granted him a continuance to await the outcome of a visa petition filed on his behalf, that he was ineffectively served by previous counsel, and that he did not knowingly waive his appeal rights. As it is not clear from the record that the respondent was properly advised of his right to appeal, we will consider the current appeal.
 
The Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent a further continuance, as the respondent did not make a persuasive showing of good cause for the request. An Immigration Judge is not obligated to suspend properly instituted removal proceedings indefinitely so that an alien can attempt to establish eligibility for relief, particularly in a case such as this where the outcome is speculative. The respondent blames his previous attorney for the length of these proceedings (Resp. Br. at 8). However, it is clear from the record that the respondent has failed to appear on several occasions before the Immigration Judge. Two orders of removal have been entered in absentia and his attorney appeared without the respondent at one time. Further the respondent appears to have missed interviews concerning the visa petitions. Tr. at 5, 8-10, 18-19, 35-36. In any event, despite his claim on appeal that the outcome of the visa petitions was not known, there was agreement below that the first two visa petitions filed on his behalf were denied (Tr. at 13, 35-36, 41-46). He argues that his previous attorney lost the evidence of a third filing, but the record still contains no documentary evidence that this petition was filed or approved. Upon de novo review, we conclude the Immigration Judge did not err in denying what would have been, at least, the respondent’s seventh continuance request concerning the visa petition. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii). We also note that nearly 2 years have elapsed since the Immigration Judge entered his decision in this case and we have received no indication that the respondent’s latest visa petition has been approved.
 
*2 Further, the respondent has failed to establish a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel which would warrant a remand of the record. The respondent has complied with many of the requirements set out in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Sako v. Gonzales, 434 F.3d (6th Cir. 2006); Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009). However, even assuming that the respondent has established that his former counsel was ineffective, he has failed to show any prejudice from the actions of his prior counsel. See id; Hamid v.Ashcroft, 336 F.3d 465 (6th Cir. 2003). That is, he has not shown that the outcome of his case may have been different absent the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. Ortiz-Puentes v. Holder, 662 F.3d 481 (8th Cir. 2011) (respondent needs to show reasonable probability that, but for the alleged errors by counsel, the result of the case would be different), citing, Obleshchenko v. Ashcroft, 392 F.3d 970, 972 (8th Cir.2004) (same). The respondent conceded he was removable as charged and has not demonstrated eligibility for any relief from removal. See Matter of Almanza, 24 I&N Dec. 771 (BIA 2009) (burden on respondent to demonstrate eligibility for relief). The respondent’s charges of ineffective assistance of counsel do not undermine the Immigration Judge’s determination of removability and the respondent’s ineligibility for adjustment of status. The respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim, therefore, fails.
 
As the Immigration Judge properly denied the continuance request and the respondent has not demonstrated any prejudice from the actions of previous counsel, the appeal will be dismissed. As we have accepted the respondent’s appeal, we will reinstate voluntary departure for a period of 60 days. We caution the respondent, however, of the severe consequences of failing to depart the United States pursuant to a grant of this relief.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
*3 NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty as provided by the regulations and the statute and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d)of the Act.
 
WARNING: If the respondent files a motion to reopen or reconsider prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period set forth above, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated; the period allowed for voluntary departure is not stayed, tolled, or extended. If the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated upon the filing of a motion, the penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(e)(1).
 
WARNING: If, prior to departing the United States, the respondent files any judicial challenge to this administratively final order, such as a petition for review pursuant to section 242 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated, and the alternate order of removal shall immediately take effect. However, if the respondent files a petition for review and then departs the United States within 30 days of such filing, the respondent will not be deemed to have departed under an order of removal if the alien provides to the DHS such evidence of his or her departure that the Immigration and Customs Enforcement Field Office Director of the DHS may require and provides evidence DHS deems sufficient that he or she has remained outside of the United States. The penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply to an alien who files a petition for review, notwithstanding any period of time that he or she remains in the United States while the petition for review is pending. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(i).
 
Michael J. Creppy

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DONOVAN FRITZ BONNER
File: AXXX XX0 009 - Miami, FL
January 25, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Joan M. Powers, Esquire1
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Brandon J. Josephsen
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely and number-barred and will be denied. The Board entered the final administrative order in this case on May 8, 2001, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reopen and rescind an in absentia removal order entered on June 12, 2000. A motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision and only one may be filed. Sections 240(c)(7)(A) and (C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(7)(A) and (C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The current motion was filed more than 10 years after the Board’s decision. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the motion.
 
The respondent seeks to reopen these proceedings to pursue adjustment of status. However, a motion to reopen to seek adjustment of status does not fall within any of the statutory or regulatory exceptions to the motions time and number limits. Sections 240(c)(7)(C)(ii)-(iv) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3).
 
Additionally, the respondent has not set forth “exceptional circumstances” that would warrant the exercise of our sua sponte authority to reopen. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (stating that “as a general matter, we invoke our sua sponte authority sparingly, treating it not as a general remedy for any hardships created by enforcement of time and number limits in the motions regulations, but as an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations”). Notably, the respondent’s potential eligibility for adjustment of status did not arise until his mother became a naturalized United States citizen on August 3, 2001, nearly 3 years after these proceedings commenced. Additionally, the visa petition (Form I-130) filed on the respondent’s behalf was not approved until March 19, 2007 nearly 6 years after the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal. Becoming potentially eligible for relief, such as adjustment of status, after a final administrative order has been entered is common and does not, in itself, constitute an exceptional circumstance warranting consideration of an untimely motion to reopen.
 
*2 To the extent that the respondent may be alleging ineffective assistance of prior counsel, the claim has not been meaningfully raised, where the respondent has not shown compliance with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). Nor has the respondent shown due diligence or prejudice, where he has waited more than 10 years to assert the claim, and has not demonstrated how previous counsel’s alleged deficient performance prejudiced the respondent’s case. See Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1274 (11th Cir. 2005) (concluding that an alien must show prejudice when he advances an ineffective assistance of counsel claim and that “[p]rejudice exists when the performance of counsel is so inadequate that there is a reasonable probability that but for the attorney’s error, the outcome of the proceedings would have been different”); Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309, 311 (BIA 1998) (alien must show that “the conduct of former counsel was so egregious that it rendered [the] hearing unfair”). We also note that the respondent’s discussion of new guidelines for prosecutorial discretion does not warrant reopening, as the exercise of prosecutorial discretion is a matter for the DHS rather than the Board.2 See Respondent’s Response in Support of the Motion at 4.
 
Accordingly, the motion to reopen will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

2

The respondent can submit a request for the favorable exercise of prosecutorial discretion directly to the DHS.

IN RE: MARTIN ROZARIO

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Paul A. Suhr, Esquire
